 303: 'immediate' consciousness of God...we must neither exaggerate its
 304: outcome nor make perverse claims about it and lessen its worth.” 
 305: Heidegger pg. 313<br>
 306: <br>
 307: 	“If the interpretation continues in
 308: this direction, one supplies a possessor for the power thus posited,
 309: or one takes the power itself as a person who makes himself known-
 310: namely God.  On the other hand one may try to reject this explanation
 311: in which the caller is taken as an alien manifestation of such a
 312: power, and to explain away the conscience 'biologically' at the same
 313: time. Both these explanations pass over the phenomenal findings too
 314: hastily.”  Heidegger pg. 320<br>
 315: <br>
 316: 	“Only when death is
 317: conceived in its full ontological essence can we have any
 318: methodological assurance in even asking what may be after death; only
 319: then can we do so with meaning and justification. Whether such a
 320: question is a possible theoretical question at all will not be
 321: decided here.”  Heidegger pg. 292<br>
 322: <br>
 323: 	“This demands that
 324: we transform our human being into its openness (dread effects this
 325: transformation in us) so that we can grasp the nothing that shows up
 326: in dread exactly as it shows up. It also demands that we expressly
 327: avoid all characterizations of the nothing that do not come from a
 328: corresponding experience of the nothing. (my emphasis)” 
 329: Heidegger<br>
 330: <br>
 331: 	This suggests that the term 'religious' must be
 332: avoided, or re-defined, due to its necessary vagueness, and due to
 333: its inevitably of being dragged down into the world of pragmatism and
 334: self-concern that goes by the name of 'religious' today. 
 335: Furthermore, it must also reject a sort of 'lyricism' of that found
 336: within mystic traditions.  Rather, the more 'neutral' term
 337: 'Transcendence' will be substituted in its place.  'Transcendence'
 338: here need not be concerned with anything accept that which can be
 339: referred to as Wholly-Other.  In other words, as suggestive of a
 340: feature of human experience that is not entirely deconstructive or
 341: naturalistic.  It must also be made clear that Transcendence is not
 342: associated with the 'supernatural', in any 'spooky' sense, due to
 343: this term also containing many specifically mystical/mythical
 344: 'religious' connotations, e.g. heaven, hell, demons, angels, and so
 345: on, that need not concern us here.  The Nihilistic experience is one,
 346: we claim, that holds, or lends itself, to a Transcendent
 347: interpretation, yet is equally founded, with equal powers of
 348: convincing, within the dogmas of Naturalism.<br>
 349: <br>
 350: 	“The void
 351: allows us to erode the idea of being; but it is not drawn into this
 352: erosion itself; it survives the an attack which would be
 353: self-destructive for any other idea.”  Cioran<br>
 354: <br>
 355: 	“If the
 356: myth is understood literally, philosophy must reject it as absurd. 
 357: It must demythologize the sacred stories, transform the myth into a
 358: philosophy of religion and finally into a philosophy without
 359: religion.”  Tillich<br>
 360: 	<br>
 361: 	We claim the various depths of
 362: Nihilism are the ground of all Transcendent interpretations of human
 363: experience.  This perennial experience is found in abundance, yet any
 364: particular 'path' or 'solution' proposed by any individual, when
 365: confronted with Nihilism, will be ignored and seen as a distraction
 366: from any 'message' that may be found within the 'reality' of
 367: Nihilism.  There is no escaping from Nihilism, there is no
 368: 'completion' within this world.  The finite is indefinitely separated
 369: from the Infinite.  All confusions, whether philosophical,
 370: naturalistic, or religious, stem from attempts of moving past the
 371: Nihilistic experience. It would therefore be prudent to heed
 372: Heidegger's words.<br>
 373: 	Behind all the dogmas, rituals, ceremonies,
 374: and assorted 'practical' modes of behavior normally found within most
 375: of the worlds religions, there is a common ground that is, for the
 376: most part, consciously or intuitively covered up (no one is
 377: completely free from experiencing Nihilism).  The 'practical'
 378: behaviors end up as another distraction from the universal nature of
 379: Nihilism.  The worldly forms of religion, when taken as ends in
 380: themselves, when taken literally, are indistinguishable from a wholly
 381: materialist, naturalistic worldview.  The finite language</div>
 382: <div> of
 383: human beings cannot ever be in complete grasp of the Other.  The
 384: misguided presumption of 'sacred', as is given the label to such
 385: 'religious' movements, must be recognized solely in their
 386: subjectivity; and even then within the acknowledgment of a complete
 387: skepticism.  The same criticism holds for philosophy and everyday
 388: life with its worldly pursuits.  Any 'meaning' that is built upon a
 389: material, transitory structure will inevitably collapse as illusory
 390: (regardless if it collapses for any one individual).  <br>
 391: <br>
 392: 	“Any
 393: fool can talk. Even parrots talk. Talking is one thing, and realising
 394: is another. Philosophies, and doctrines, and arguments, and books,
 395: and theories, and churches, and sects, and all these things are good
 396: in their own way; but when that realisation comes, these things drop
 397: away.” Swami Vivekananda<br>
 398: <br>
 399: 	“In true faith the ultimate
 400: concern is a concern about the truly ultimate; while in idolatrous
 401: faith preliminary, finite realities are elevated to the rank of
 402: ultimacy.  The inescapable consequence of idolatrous faith is
 403: 'existential disappointment,' a disappointment which penetrates into
 404: the very existence of man!”  Tillich<br>
 405: 	<br>
 406: 	For those who
 407: have not suffered the overwhelming experience of Nothingness, for
 408: those who still find 'something' within the world, these words will
 409: more than likely be tossed away and labeled as 'whining and pouting'.
 410:  To interpret a Taoist saying into the relevance of Nihilism: the
 411: common people will laugh at it, the 'intellectual' will understand
 412: but not experience, and the one who experiences will be left to the
 413: Nothingness of the world.<br>
 414: <br>
 415: 	“the It world...one has to
 416: live and also can live comfortably- and that even offers us all sorts
 417: of stimulations and excitements, activities and knowledge. In this
 418: firm and wholesome chronicle the You-moments appear as queer
 419: lyric-dramatic episodes.”  Buber<br>
 420: <br>
 421: 	“But along with this
 422: tranquilization, which forces Dasein away from its death, the “they”
 423: at the same time puts itself in the right and makes itself
 424: respectable by tacitly regulating the way in which one has to comport
 425: oneself towards death. It is already a matter of public acceptance
 426: that ‘thinking about death’ is a cowardly fear, a sign of
 427: insecurity on the part of Dasein, and a sombre way of fleeing from
 428: the world. The “they” does not permit us the courage for anxiety
 429: in the face of death.”  Heidegger<br>
 430: <br>
 431: 	“This ‘movement’
 432: of Dasein in its own Being, we call its “downward plunge”. Dasein
 433: plunges out of itself into itself, into the groundlessness and
 434: nullity of inauthentic everydayness. But this plunge remains hidden
 435: from Dasein by the way things have been publicly interpreted, so much
 436: so, indeed, that it gets interpreted as a way of ‘ascending’ and
 437: ‘living concretely’.”  Heidegger<br>
 438: <br>
 439: 	If Nihilism is, as
 440: we claim, the ground for all Transcendent thinking, we must first put
 441: an emphasis on the direct experience of Nihilism.  Swami
 442: Vivekananda's words speak to this: <br>
 443: <br>
 444: 	“In one form or
 445: another, we are all in it. It is a most difficult and intricate state
 446: of things to understand. It has been preached in every country,
 447: taught everywhere, but only believed in by a few, because until we
 448: get the experiences ourselves we cannot believe in it (my emphasis).
 449: What does it show? Something very terrible. For it is all futile.
 450: Time, the avenger of everything, comes, and nothing is left. He
 451: swallows up the saint and the sinner, the king and the peasant, the
 452: beautiful and the ugly; he leaves nothing. Everything is rushing
 453: towards that one goal, destruction. Our knowledge, our arts, our
 454: sciences, everything is rushing towards it. None can stem the tide,
 455: none can hold it back for a minute. We may try to forget it, in the
 456: same way that persons in a plague-stricken city try to create
 457: oblivion by drinking, dancing, and other vain attempts, and so
 458: becoming paralysed. So we are trying to forget, trying to create
 459: oblivion by all sorts of sense-pleasures.”  <br>
 460: <br>
 461: 	Nietzsche,
 462: the so-called 'Nihilistic-Prophet', also stresses the need for the
 463: experience of Nihilism.  In his words: “...because nihilism
 464: represents the ultimate logical conclusion of our great values and
 465: ideals— because we must experience nihilism before we can find out
 466: what value these 'values' really had.”<br>
 467: <br>
 468: 	”It might seem
 469: frightful to any one who does not realize the nothingness and
 470: absurdity of an isolated personal life, and who believes that he will
 471: never die. But I know that my life, considered in relation to my
 472: individual happiness, is, taken by itself, a stupendous farce, and
 473: that this meaningless existence will end in a stupid death.” 
 474: Tolstoy<br>
 475: <br>
 476: 	“Suddenly every vain hope became worthless to
 477: me.”  St. Augustine<br>
 478: <br>
 479: 	Tønnessen supplies a vivid example
 480: of the primacy of experience: <br>
 481: <br>
 482: 	“Another question...is
 483: the question of whether such insights can be taught...The so-called
 484: 'engaged' discourse introduced in heart-philosophy admits of a third
 485: component which we may tentatively designate the degree of
 486: integration of knowledge.  An example will indicate what may be meant
 487: by the expressions “integrated” and “integration”...the Finns
 488: caught a Russian spy...He knew the outcome...When finally the death
 489: sentence is pronounced, he completely collapses.  What on earth
 490: happened?  He knew the outcome with absolute certainty.  We should
 491: want to say the spy knows about his immanent death now, in a new and
 492: terrifying way.  He has suddenly obtained an insight, a knowledge
 493: which penetrates him, goes through bones and marrow and violently
 494: shakes up the total personality structure into its deepest and
 495: darkest labyrinths.  Unfortunately, this “integration” of
 496: knowledge cannot be taught in any ordinary sense of
 497: teaching.”<br>
 498: 	<br>
 499: 	There is a hidden meaningless throughout
 500: all of the worlds activities that are drawn out from such an
 501: experience of Nihilism.  But before going too far, what do we mean
 502: when we speak of an 'experience of Nihilism'?  We must first lay out
 503: the ontological basis of this Nothingness, which can be done in
 504: various forms.  Only then will there be any possibility of conceiving
 505: the development of a language of Nihilism.  Due to my lack of
 506: creativity, I will quote, at length, Tønnessen's graphic depiction
 507: of such an experience: <br>
 508: <br>
 509: 	“They have a feeling of
 510: integral selfhood, of personal identity, and of the permanency of
 511: things.  They believe in their own continuity – in being made of
 512: good, lasting stuff – and in meaning and order and justice in life
 513: and in the universe.  In the most fortunate cases, there is a good,
 514: healthy unconditional surrender and submission to the norms of nicety
 515: and normalcy of the average, square-headed, stuffed-shirted,
 516: sanctimonious, middle-class North-American church-goer and bridge
 517: player, with his pseudo-intelligent, quasi-progressive,
 518: simili-cultured, platitudinal small-talk.  Happy days! In this the
 519: best of all possible worlds. One doesn't notice until too late.  In
 520: short: All is well (since nobody notices the end of 'all that is
 521: well') until one night: the day's work is well done and all the
 522: ships' crapulant fools frantically engulf themselves in a deadly
 523: serious game of bridge (till it is time for the night-cap and the
 524: tranquillizer).  One of the 'dummies,' a champion brass polisher,
 525: suffering from an acute case of uncaused depression, goes to lie down
 526: for a while; he doesn't have a dime for the jukebox; the room is
 527: painfully satiated with embarrassing silence.  Instantly and
 528: unexpectedly he is struck by an execrative curse of inverted
 529: serendipity.  He suddenly, in unbearable agony, sees himself as an
 530: upholstered pile of bones and knuckles, with the softer parts slung
 531: up in a bad on the front side, and his whole like as a ludicrously
 532: brief interlude between embryo and corpse, two repulsive caricatures
 533: of himself.  As for this flying farce, this nauseatingly trivial
 534: burlesque in a whirling coffin, and its aimless, whimsical flight
 535: through the void: 'What is it all about?' The question permeates him
 536: with dread and anguish, with 'ontological despair' and 'existential
 537: frustration.'”<br>
 538: <br>
 539: 	“In dread, as we say, "something
 540: is uncanny." What do we mean by "something" and "is"?
 541: We cannot say what the uncanny something is about. There is something
 542: like this about the "as a whole”: all things and we ourselves
 543: sink into indifference. (my emphasis)  Not in the sense of merely
 544: disappearing, but rather, in its very moving away, it turns to us.
 545: This moving away of be-ing as a whole that closes in on us in dread
 546: pressures us.  There's nothing to get a hold on.  All that remains
 547: and comes over us in the slipping away of be-ing is this "no-thing.
 548: "Dread reveals no-thing.”  Heidegger<br>
 549: <br>
 550: 	“In
 551: particular, that in the face of which one has anxiety is not
 552: encountered as something definite with which one can concern onself;
 553: the threatening does not come from what is ready-to-hand or
 554: present-at-hand, but rather from the fact that neither of these
 555: 'says' anything any longer. Environmental entities no longer have any
 556: involvement.  The world in which I exist has sunk into insignificance
 557: (my emphasis); and the world which is thus disclosed is one in which
 558: entities can be freed only in the character of having no involvement.
 559:  Anxiety is anxious in the face of the nothing of the world (my
 560: emphasis); but this does not mean that in anxiety we experience
 561: something like the absence of what is present-at-hand with-the-world.
 562: The present-at-hand must be encountered in just such a way that it
 563: does not have any involvement whatsoever, but can show itself in an
 564: empty mercilessness.”  Heidegger<br>
 565: <br>
 566: 	We may also express
 567: Nihilism in the form of a question: first, put into your mind the
 568: most meaningful 'thing' of your existence, whether this is family, a
 569: project, a cause, a nation, or even 'oneself'.  Now, can you imagine
 570: what you hold to be most meaningful, the thing that makes your
 571: existence come across as a real possibility, as being worthless, as
 572: utterly void of all meaning?  I have</div>
 573: <div> yet
 574: to meet a person with even a slight indication of what this question
 575: is referring to.  To answer in the affirmative is to have experienced
 576: a deconstructive-terror that runs through one's entire being.  There
 577: is a vanishing of any ground that one has placed under the feet of
 578: existence.  <br>
 579: 	William James puts it this way: “Conceive
 580: yourself, if possible, suddenly stripped of all the emotion with
 581: which your world now inspires you, and try to imagine it AS IT
 582: EXISTS, purely by itself, without your favorable or unfavorable,
 583: hopeful or apprehensive comment. It will be almost impossible for you
 584: to realize such a condition of negativity and deadness.”  And
 585: although Nihilism is found universally within the human condition,
 586: the experience is still only to be received in piecemeal, which
 587: informs us on one possible reason for why its extreme forms are so
 588: rare.  <br>
 589: <br>
 590: 	“In great despair, for example, when all weight
 591: tends to dwindle away from things and the sense of things grows dark,
 592: the question looms.”  Heidegger<br>
 593: <br>
 594: 	 “If existence only
 595: relates itself to being by being aimed in advance at no-thing in
 596: order to be able to exist, and if no-thing originally becomes
 597: manifest only in dread, must we not then remain permanently suspended
 598: in this dread in order to be able to exist at all? Yet have we
 599: ourselves not already admitted that this original dread is rare? But
 600: above all, all of us exist and relate ourselves to being which we
 601: ourselves are not and which we ourselves are—without such dread. Is
 602: this not an arbitrary finding and the no-thing attributed to it an
 603: exaggeration?”  Heidegger<br>
 604: <br>
 605: 	“Now what does it mean that
 606: this original dread happens only in rare instances?  Nothing other
 607: than this: no-thing is at first and for the most part disguised in
 608: its originality. But how? By our getting lost in being in certain
 609: ways. The more we turn to being in our dealings, the less we let
 610: being as such slip away, the more we turn away from no-thing. Thus
 611: all the more certainly are we forced into the public superficialities
 612: of existence.”  Heidegger<br>
 613: <br>
 614: 	'but why is the experience of
 615: agony so rare? Can it be that our hypothesis is entirely false and
 616: that sketching a metaphysics of death is possible only by accepting
 617: death's transcendental nature?”  Cioran <br>
 618: <br>
 619: 	“No one is
 620: continually subject to the obsession with this horror. Sometimes we
 621: turn from it, almost forget it...”  Cioran<br>
 622: <br>
 623: 	“Now, the
 624: great majority of mankind endure life without any great protest, and
 625: believe, to this extent, in the value of existence, but that is
 626: because each individual decides and determines alone, and never comes
 627: out of his own personality like these exceptions: everything outside
 628: of the personal has no existence for them or at the utmost is
 629: observed as but a faint shadow.”  Nietzsche<br>
 630: 	<br>
 631: 	“Enter
 632: through the narrow gate. For wide is the gate and broad is the road
 633: that leads to destruction, and many enter through it.  But small is
 634: the gate and narrow the road that leads to life, and only a few find
 635: it.”  Matthew 7:13-14<br>
 636: <br>
 637: 	Even the one who experiences
 638: Nihilism in a deeper form is left with a sense of suspicion, a
 639: suspicion that this 'Nihilism' is still too foreign.  All must ask a
 640: question concerning the actual 'reality' of Nihilism along with the
 641: intuition of 'a something' that lurks behind all Nihilistic
 642: experiences.  The busyness of the world, the conscious fleeing from
 643: the human condition, the 'taking serious' of any particular worldly
 644: endeavor, the lack of uncanniness within the material world, these
 645: are ways in which one drives out, consciously or instinctually, and
 646: misses Nihilism or any 'message' within that it may convey.  The path
 647: may be narrow, but why does it seem to also be 'obstructed' to such
 648: an extreme degree?  The 'falling' nature of human beings as being
 649: wrapped up within the world, combined with the rarity of Nihilism, is
 650: an unbearable weight for the one who is seeking authenticity, for one
 651: attempting a participation with Nihilism.   <br>
 652: <br>
 653: 	(maybe
 654: Kierkegaard “double-mindedness” and James “divided-self”
 655: quotes here?)<br>
 656: <br>
 657: 	“When anxiety has subsided, then in our
 658: everyday way of talking we are accustomed to say that ‘it was
 659: really nothing’.”  Heidegger<br>
 660: 	<br>
 661: 	“Compared to
 662: philosophers, saints know nothing.  Yet they know everything.
 663: Compared to Aristotle, any saint is illiterate.  What makes us then
 664: believe that we might learn more from the latter?  Because all of the
 665: philosophers put together are not worth a single saint.  Philosophy
 666: has no answers.  Compared to philosophy, saintliness is an exact
 667: science.  It gives us precise answers to questions that philosophers
 668: do not even dare consider. Its method is suffering and its goal is
 669: God.”  Cioran<br>
 670: <br>
 671: 	One way in which Nietzsche speaks of
 672: Nihilism comes from a term he referred to as the 'Absurd Valuation'. 
 673: He puts his description in the mouth of his Zarathustra:  “What is
 674: your greatest experience? It is the hour of the great contempt. The
 675: hour in which even your happiness becomes repulsive to you, and even
 676: your reason and virtue.  The hour when you say: "What good is my
 677: happiness! It is poverty and dirt and wretched contentment. But my
 678: happiness should justify existence itself!  The hour when you say:
 679: "What good is my reason! does it long for knowledge as the lion
 680: for his food? It is poverty and dirt and wretched contentment!" 
 681: The hour when you say: "What good are my virtues?! As yet they
 682: have not made me rage with passion. How weary I am of my good and
 683: evil! It is all poverty and dirt and wretched contentment!”<br>
 684: 	I
 685: prefer the term 'Absurd Perspective'.  This is the 'revelation' of
 686: the paralyzing nature of Nihilism that comes from the absence of
 687: values, hence making all judgments, movements, efforts, concerns, and
 688: offenses as baseless as any other; we are left with any response or
 689: action being reduced to Absurdity, since humans are necessarily
 690: forced to make value judgments in a valueless universe.  All human
 691: movements are motivated by a value judgment.  Without moral values,
 692: no non-arbitrary human action can be made.  With all possibilities
 693: 'equivalent' there is no right or wrong, there is no up or down, all
 694: possibilities drop off, there is only Nothing...yet humans act in the
 695: world.<br>
 696: 	An aspect of the Absurd Perspective can in fact be
 697: described in an empirical context found within the consequences of
 698: the 'multi-verse' or 'world-assemble', a view that many modern
 699: physicists postulate as a description of 'reality'.  Regardless if
 700: the multi-verse is the 'correct' view of reality, assuming it is
 701: 'true', there are consequences that follow from such a position,
 702: which go seemingly unnoticed by both scientists and philosophers of
 703: science.  In short, if there are an infinite number of 'universes'
 704: and, in theory, an infinite number of 'yous', then any decision, in
 705: any given situation that is possible, will be made by one of 'you'. 
 706: Therefore, it is only an illusion when one finds themselves 'caught'
 707: in some form of a 'moral crisis', since no matter which option is
 708: chosen, whether you believe it to be the 'right' decision based on
 709: intuition, feelings, or from philosophical comtemplation, all other
 710: options will also be chosen, by 'you'.  This turns every 'important'
 711: choice or decision into a triviality.  Subjective morality and
 712: meaning are utterly empty, when choice is simply illusory.  This is
 713: to refrain from getting stuck in the cobwebs of 'free will' and
 714: 'determinism'.  If an infinite world-assemble is in fact our reality,
 715:  then determinism is as true as it ever could be, even if 'free
 716: will', if this concept can even be made sense of, is granted in each
 717: individual universe.  <br>
 718: 	The reply is anticipated: 'Regardless if
 719: all choices are made elsewhere, my decision still matters here, to
 720: me, in this world?”  Let's also not digress into details concerning
 721: the 'A' and 'B' theories of time.  But, we may suggest, in passing,
 722: that if we assume that the 'B' theory of time is correct, and there
 723: is no temporal becoming, there is no privileged 'now'.  Therefore,
 724: your birth is as 'real' as your death; your existence is equivalent
 725: to a movie reel that is sprawled out across the ground frame by
 726: frame.  Even with that said, hapless as it is, there is no doubt that
 727: this still does matter to you.  <br>
 728: 	We can put forth Nihilism in
 729: yet another way.  The insatiable credulity in the way in which most
 730: humans force an elevation of subjective meaning on to one's life,
 731: with a conscious denial of objectivity, shows a lack of The
 732: Experience concerning the paradoxical nature of human subjectivity
 733: and 'deep-time'.  Human minds have not 'evolved' in a way in which to
 734: properly handle the exorbitant amount of time in which science has so
 735: far discovered about the universe and the human situation within it. 
 736: Within the billions of years of evolution, the almost fourteen
 737: billion year old observable piece of space-time, humans have
 738: developed a local, restricted, 'small world' understanding of the
 739: situation they find themselves in.  This 'small world' understanding
 740: allows for most to live safely, 'out of sight-out of mind', shielded
 741: from the horrors that underpin self-conscious existence.      <br>
 742: 	The
 743: 'problem' of the denial of Nihilism, followed by the proposing of a
 744: meaning within the world of 'deep time', comes from the fact that
 745: most of human existence has been completely erased without a trace of
 746: their even 'being here', except if one wants to count what can be
 747: found of 'them' in the strands of our DNA.  <br>
 748: 	<br>
 749: 	“All
 750: civilizations become defunct. All species die out. There is even an
 751: expiration date on the universe itself...the universe as nothing in
 752: motion.”  Ligotti<br>
 753: <br>
 754: 	“A thousand varieties of man --
 755: Piltdown, Neanderthal, Chellean, Acheulean, Mousterian, Aurignacian,
 756: Cro-magnon, Rhodesian, Pekin man -- lived for thousands of years,
 757: fought, thought, invented, painted, carved, made children, and left
 758: no more to posterity than a few flints and scratches, forgotten for
 759: millennia</div>
 760: <div> and
 761: found only by the picks and spades of our inquisitive day. &nbsp; A
 762: thousand civilizations have disappeared under the ocean or the earth,
 763: leaving, like Atlantis, merely a legend behind...”  Durant<br>
 764: <br>
 765: 	“All
 766: the power of knowledge and wealth once made has passed away — all
 767: the sciences of the ancients, lost, lost forever. Nobody knows how.
 768: That teaches us a grand lesson. Vanity of vanities; all is vanity and
 769: vexation of the spirit.”   Vivekananda<br>
 770: <br>
 771: 	“...everything
 772: in the instincts that is beneficial, that fosters life and that
 773: safeguards the future is a cause of suspicion. So to live that life
 774: no longer has any meaning: this is now the "meaning" of
 775: life...Why be public-spirited? Why take any pride in descent and
 776: forefathers? Why labour together, trust one another, or concern one's
 777: self about the common welfare, and try to serve it?”  Nietzsche
 778: <br>
 779: <br>
 780: 	To pronounce everything as 'meaningless' is to show zero
 781: appreciation for every human struggle, the billions of years of
 782: conflict found within all sentient creatures, down to every
 783: replicating cell;  what a big responsibility to take on, if there was
 784: any such thing as 'responsibility'.  Is the most hubris filled act
 785: that of embracing of Nihilism?  Is the Saint, the one who renounces
 786: the entire world, nothing but the most selfish of creatures? <br>
 787: <br>
 788: 	All
 789: actions will be erased.  All accomplishments will disappear.  All
 790: cares, concerns, achievements, disappointments, and failures will be
 791: as if they never occurred.  With this fact of existence, why is it
 792: that humans still insist on thrusting a sense of worldly meaning onto
 793: their individual lives?  Why can someone sit there with a smile on
 794: their face, proclaim 'But my life is meaningful!', in the face of the
 795: consequences of 'deep time', and not be seen as psychologically or
 796: intellectually deficient?  The only way to justify such thought and
 797: behavior, in a universe that itself will eventually die in ruins, is
 798: to maintain some neurotic form of justification for the value of
 799: being alive.  How does one justify their stance of intrinsic value on
 800: life, no matter what (for now stepping back from the proponents of
 801: euthanasia or the proclamations of many 'theistic' believers)?  The
 802: 'logic' of life, that existence is 'better' than non-existence, has
 803: not only proceeded without justification, it is rarely even
 804: questioned.  Worldly 'meaning' is dead. Yet there is no 'argument' to
 805: convince another against being able to find meaning within the world.
 806:  One must experience the Nothing of the world directly.<br>
 807: <br>
 808: 	“...there
 809: are two inevitable conditions of life, confronting all of us, which
 810: destroy its whole meaning; (1) death, which may at any moment pounce
 811: upon each of us; and (2) the transitoriness of all our works, which
 812: so soon pass away and leave no trace...And therefore, however we may
 813: conceal it from ourselves, we cannot help seeing that the
 814: significance of our life cannot lie in our personal fleshly
 815: existence...”  Tolstoy<br>
 816: 	<br>
 817: 	An illustration may bring some
 818: additional clarity, even if it does lack any force of convincing. 
 819: Imagine you wake up one day and find yourself inside of a gated
 820: tennis court.  You are there with another person, racket in hand, and
 821: the gate to the court cannot be opened, there is no escape.  Your
 822: partner happens to mention that there is no tennis ball, yet insists
 823: on 'playing'.  Would you consent to this game of 'tennis'?  Would you
 824: 'go through the motions' of swinging mindlessly at a phantom ball
 825: that does not exist?  Would you argue with your partner who takes a
 826: swing at the 'ball' and asserts on his scoring a point?  Would you
 827: regard the 'meaning' that your partner finds in this game worthy of
 828: the label 'meaningful'?  If you could invent some form of subjective
 829: meaning to this 'game', how long would it keep you content?  And can
 830: you change your mind, many times over, on the 'meaning' of this game?
 831:  How long would you stay motivated to participate?  At what point
 832: would you give up?  Would anyone want, or be able, to participate in
 833: such an obscene activity?   Can anyone, who's not simply being
 834: argumentative or denialistic, claim any 'meaning' behind such an
 835: activity?  Anyone perceiving the situation properly would have to
 836: insist on not participating in such a ridiculous situation.  But,
 837: then, what?<br>
 838: <br>
 839: 	“Genuine boredom has not yet arrived if we
 840: are merely bored with this book or that movie, with this job or that
 841: idle moment. Genuine boredom occurs when one’s whole world is
 842: boring. Then abysmal boredom, like a muffling fog, drifts where it
 843: will in the depths of our openness, sucking everything and everyone,
 844: and ourselves along with them, into a numbing sameness. This kind of
 845: boredom reveals what-is in terms of a whole.”  Heidegger<br>
 846: <br>
 847: 	Nihilism
 848: takes the 'ball' away from the game of life.  Each new 'game' that is
 849: invented eventually succumbs to absurdity and boredom.  One
 850: constantly finds new things 'to do', yet are they ever fully
 851: satisfied?  To wonder as Pascal did: why can't humans sit alone in a
 852: room?  I'll let Pascal expand on this observation: <br>
 853: <br>
 854: 	“Nothing
 855: is so insufferable to man as to be completely at rest, without
 856: passions, without business, without diversion, without study. He then
 857: feels his nothingness, his forlornness, his insufficiency, his
 858: dependence, his weakness, his emptiness. There will immediately arise
 859: from the depth of his heart weariness, gloom, sadness, fretfulness,
 860: vexation, despair.” <br>
 861: 	<br>
 862: 	This fact, that man is always
 863: attempting to do something, shows that there is some search he is on.
 864:  Is this purely a natural instinct to survive and reproduce?  A 'will
 865: to live' for no Ultimate reason?  This Transcendental yearning, this
 866: pull towards the Infinite, instead of being confronted, is almost
 867: always translated into some worldly activity.  There is nothing to
 868: find in this world.  People want Simple Salvation; no one wants to
 869: participate in the Nothingness.  <br>
 870: <br>
 871: 	“Hiding facts is not
 872: the way to find a remedy. As you all know, a hare hunted by dogs puts
 873: its head down and thinks itself safe; so, when we run into optimism;
 874: we do just like the hare, but that is no remedy.” 
 875: Vivekananda<br>
 876: <br>
 877: 	“Everything that we do to make our
 878: existence secure is like the act of the ostrich, when she hides her
 879: head in the sand, and does not see that her destruction is near. But
 880: we are even more foolish than the ostrich.”  Tolstoy<br>
 881: <br>
 882: 	I
 883: see a world of human bodies, scampering around in ignorant-confusion,
 884: within the illusion of confidence, all with their own little, black
 885: cloud of death hovering over their heads.  “What a Meaningful
 886: life!”, shouted the man who's heart only seconds later unknowingly
 887: delivered him into Nothingness.  He's already a memory.<br>
 888: 	Tolstoy
 889: captures his realization of Nihilism as follows: <br>
 890: <br>
 891: 	“I
 892: was only astonished that this had not occurred to me before, from
 893: premises which had so long been known. Illness and death would come
 894: (indeed they had come), if not to-day, then to-morrow, to those whom
 895: I loved, to myself, and nothing would remain but stench and worms.
 896: All my acts, whatever I did, would sooner or later be forgotten, and
 897: I myself be nowhere. Why, then, busy one's self with anything? How
 898: could men see this and live? It is possible to live only as long as
 899: life intoxicates us; as soon as we are sober again we see that it is
 900: all a delusion, and a stupid one! In this, indeed, there is nothing
 901: either ludicrous or amusing; it is only cruel and absurd.”  (pg.
 902: 282 journal, Buddha, Pascal, Vivekananda)<br>
 903: <br>
 904: 	If the world is
 905: Nothing, and is experienced as such, what is left?  Madness, suicide,
 906: or the Other.  The 'message' of Nihilism may not be wholly
 907: deconstructive, yet, at the same time, is the 'More' of Nihilism
 908: essentially unknowable?  What is the 'More' of Nihilism?  Are
 909: 'madness, suicide, and the Other' all that distinct?  Does madness
 910: lead to suicide, or to the Other?  Does the Other lead to Suicide? 
 911: To develop a language or philosophy of Nihilism leaves open the
 912: possibility that it may indeed lead to, or find no distinction in,
 913: madness, suicide or the Other.<br>
 914: <br>
 915: 	“And indeed the
 916: Christian is, in a certain sense--in an ultimate sense--a "Nihilist";
 917: for to him, in the end, the world is nothing, and God is all. This
 918: is, of course, the precise opposite of the Nihilism we have examined
 919: here, where God is nothing and the world is all; that is a Nihilism
 920: that proceeds from the Abyss, and the Christian's is a "Nihilism"
 921: that proceeds from abundance.”  Fr.  Sarephim Rose <br>
 922: 	“in
 923: such moments you will be severed from life, from love, smiles,
 924: friends and even from death. and you will ask yourself if there is
 925: anything besides the nothingness of the world and your own
 926: nothingness.”  Cioran<br>
 927: <br>
 928: 	“But then, Nothingness is only
 929: Nothingness. How did it happen to turn into Something? And once
 930: having become Something, how did it acquire such limitless power over
 931: man, and even over all existence?”  Shestov<hr><h2><em>The Uncanny Illusion of Naturalism</em>&nbsp;</h2><h3>-Awaken, Material Nightmare-</h3><br>
 932: <br>
 933: 	“I know that I must either find a way to move
 934: beyond the theistic patterns of the past in search for a new way to
 935: speak of and to engage the ultimate reality that I call God, or be
 936: honest about living in a godless world.”  Spong<br>
 937: <br>
 938: 	“...even
 939: if God did exist, that would change nothing.” 
 940: Sartre<br>
 941: 	<br>
 942: 	“...practical men tell us, 'don't bother your
 943: heads with such nonsense as religion and metaphysics.  Live here;
 944: this is a very bad world indeed, but make the best of it.'  Which put
 945: in plain language means, live a hypocritical, lying life, a life of
 946: continuous fraud, covering all sores in the best way you can. Go on
 947: putting patch after patch, until everything is lost, and you are a
 948: mass of patchwork. This is what is called practical life. Those that
 949: are satisfied with this patchwork will never come to religion.
 950: Religion begins with a tremendous dissatisfaction with the present
 951: state of things, with our lives, and a hatred, an intense hatred, for
 952: this patching up of life, an unbounded disgust</div>
 953: <div> for
 954: fraud and lies. He alone can be religious who dares say, as the
 955: mighty Buddha once said under the Bo-tree, when this idea of
 956: practicality appeared before him and he saw that it was nonsense, and
 957: yet could not find a way out.”  Vivekananda<br>
 958: <br>
 959: 	Many have
 960: spoken of the Nihilistic experience properly, but almost all turn
 961: their back from Nihilism and flee into some activity of distraction. 
 962: As Heidegger says, one is always falling back into the world. 
 963: <br>
 964: <br>
 965: 	“Throwness is neither a 'fact that is finished' nor a
 966: fact that is settled.  Dasein's facticity is such that as long as it
 967: is what it is, Dasein remains in the throw, and is sucked into the
 968: turbulence of the 'They's' inauthenticty.” pg. 223<br>
 969: <br>
 970: 	“In
 971: everydayness Dasein can undergo dull ‘suffering’, sink away in
 972: the dullness of it, and evade it by seeking new ways in which its
 973: dispersion in its affairs may be further dispersed. In the moment of
 974: vision, indeed, and often just ‘for that moment’, existence can
 975: even gain the mastery over the “everyday”; but it can never
 976: extinguish it.”  Heidegger<br>
 977: <br>
 978: 	“Nothing is so plain from
 979: the course of the existential analytic so far, as the Fact that the
 980: ontology of Dasein is always falling back upon the allurements of the
 981: way in which Being ordinarily understood.”  Heidegger pg.
 982: 439<br>
 983: <br>
 984: 	This divided-self, this double-mindedness, is one in
 985: which there is no escape.  To make clear, this 'always falling' has
 986: been, at least within Western, Christian cultures, painted with the
 987: mythological language of 'original sin' (or with a scientific,
 988: evolutionary tale that reduces consciousness and all of its
 989: phenomenons as a mistake or accident).  This unbridgeable disconnect
 990: between the finite and the Infinite has been excepted only
 991: 'intellectually' while shunned, in action, by almost all with their
 992: 'solutions' and 'answers', except the rare few who have the 'stamina'
 993: to renounce the world in its entirety and who hold that reality is
 994: simply an unreality.  <br>
 995: <br>
 996: 	“Meanwhile time passes. If
 997: outward help comes, then life returns to the despairer, he begins
 998: where he left off; he had no self, and a self he did not become, but
 999: he continues to live on with only the quality of immediacy. If
1000: outward help does not come, then in real life something else commonly
1001: occurs. Life comes back into him after all, but "he never will
1002: be himself again," so he says. He now acquires some little
1003: understanding of life, he learns to imitate the other men, noting how
1004: they manage to live, and so he too lives after a sort. In Christendom
1005: he too is a Christian, goes to church every Sunday, hears and
1006: understands the parson, yea, they understand one another; he dies;
1007: the parson introduces him into eternity for the price of $10 -- but a
1008: self he was not, and a self he did not become.” Kierkegaard<br>
1009: <br>
1010: 	“A
1011: thinker erects an immense building, a system, a system which embraces
1012: the whole of existence and world-history etc. -- and if we
1013: contemplate his personal life, we discover to our astonishment this
1014: terrible and ludicrous fact, that he himself personally does not live
1015: in this immense high-vaulted palace, but in a barn alongside of it,
1016: or in a dog kennel, or at the most in the porter's lodge. If one were
1017: to take the liberty of calling his attention to this by a single
1018: word, he would be offended. For he has no fear of being under a
1019: delusion, if only he can get the system completed...by means of the
1020: delusion.”  Kierkegaard <br>
1021: <br>
1022: 	“In spite of the fact that a
1023: man is in despair he can perfectly well live on in the temporal, in
1024: fact all the better for it; he may be praised by men, be honored and
1025: esteemed, and pursue all the aims of temporal life. What is called
1026: worldliness is made up of just such men, who (if one may use the
1027: expression) pawn themselves to the world. They use their talents,
1028: accumulate money, carry on worldly affairs, calculate shrewdly, etc.,
1029: etc., are perhaps mentioned in history, but themselves they are not;
1030: spiritually understood, they have no self, no self for whose sake
1031: they could venture everything, no self before God -- however selfish
1032: they may be for all that.”  Kierkegaard <br>
1033: <br>
1034: 	“Is not
1035: despair simply double-mindedness? For what is despairing other than
1036: to have two wills?” Kierkegaard<br>
1037: <br>
1038: 	“Thus did my two
1039: wills, one new, and the other old, one carnal, the other spiritual,
1040: struggle within me; and by their discord, undid (tore) my soul
1041: (apart).” St. Augustine<br>
1042: <br>
1043: 	“...give me beauty in the
1044: inward soul; and may the outward and inward man be at one.” 
1045: Plato<br>
1046: <br>
1047: 	“Two enemies- the same man divided.” 
1048: Cioran<br>
1049: <br>
1050: 	“Natural good is not simply insufficient in
1051: amount and transient, there lurks a falsity in its very being. 
1052: Cancelled as it all is by death if not by earlier enemies, it gives
1053: no final balance, and can never be the thing intended for our lasting
1054: worship. It keeps us from our real good, rather; and renunciation and
1055: despair of it are our first step in the direction of the truth. There
1056: are two lives, the natural and the spiritual, and we must lose the
1057: one before we can participate in the other.”  James<br>
1058: <br>
1059: 	“I
1060: and You drown; humanity that but now confronted the deity is absorbed
1061: into it; glorification, deification, universal unity have appeared. 
1062: But when one returns into the wretchedness of daily turmoil,
1063: transfigured and exhausted, and with a knowing heart reflects on
1064: both, is one not bound to feel that Being is split, with one part
1065: abandoned to hopelessness?”  Buber<br>
1066: <br>
1067: 	“Nature her self,
1068: apparently, will torment thee, she being always an Enemy to the
1069: Spirit, which in depriving her of sensible Pleasures, remains Weak,
1070: Melancholy, and full of Irksomeness, so that it feels a Hell in all
1071: Spiritual Exercises, particularly in that of Prayer, hence it grows
1072: extremely impatient to be at an end of it, through the uneasiness of
1073: Thoughts, the lassitude of Body, importunate Sleep, and the not being
1074: able to curb the Senses, every one of which would for it own share,
1075: follow its own Pleasure.”  Molinos<br>
1076: <br>
1077: 	A Jew, a Christian,
1078: a Nihilist.  There's a 'Hindu' in there too somewhere. <br>
1079: 	<br>
1080: 	The
1081: naturalistic side of humanity, which constantly pulls one down into
1082: the world, into the concerns of the finite, into taking serious the
1083: transitory nature of all worldly things, cannot be overcome.  The
1084: divided-self, the falling nature of man, the 'pulling' in contrary
1085: directions as existing as both simultaneously natural and
1086: Transcendent, is one that comes with great insights as part of the
1087: human condition.  This split within the human consciousness helps
1088: explain the way in which people cannot live up to the ideals of
1089: philosophy/theology/religion etc. and are doomed to be pulled back
1090: down, in all its depravity, into the world leaving one with a feeling
1091: of 'being guilty'.<br>
1092: <br>
1093: 	“The common sense of the “They”
1094: knows only the satisfying of manipulable rules and public norms and
1095: the failure to satisfy them. It reckons up infractions of them and
1096: tries to balance them off. It has slunk away from its ownmost
1097: Being-guilty so as to be able to talk more loudly about making
1098: “mistakes”...Though the call gives no information, it is not
1099: merely critical; it is positive, in that it discloses Dasein’s most
1100: primordial potentiality-for-Being as Being-guilty..” 
1101: Heidegger<br>
1102: <br>
1103: 	There is a never ending frustration within the
1104: divided nature of human consciousness for which the consequence is
1105: described as guilt.  The conception of 'guilt', under Heidegger's
1106: analysis, gets rescued from a worldly stuckness of a feeling of owing
1107: something to someone else or not living up to another's worldly
1108: ideals or 'oughts'.  'Guilt', in its existential sense, stems from
1109: the 'always falling' condition, as being dragged around by a pure
1110: naturalism, as not even attempting to recognize the possibility of
1111: one's True-Self.  Guilt is not for others, guilt is directed at
1112: oneself in the frustrated state of the Natural man who, as Becker
1113: puts it, is both 'worm and god'; a 'god who shits'.  In Nihilism,
1114: others become insignificant and the Original Self becomes one's
1115: fascination; there is no longer a feeling of 'owing' anyone or
1116: anything.  How different, then, is this Self, this inner Transcendent
1117: intuition that 'separates' itself from the purely Natural side of
1118: man, from the Other?  Are they one in the same?<br>
1119: <br>
1120: 	 “on
1121: the heights of despair, the passion for the absurd is the only thing
1122: that can still throw a demonic light on chaos.  When all the current
1123: reasons- moral, esthetic, religious, social, and so on - no longer
1124: guide one's life, how can one sustain life without succumbing to
1125: nothingness?”  Cioran<br>
1126: <br>
1127: 	This 'fascination' with the
1128: Transcendent side of the Self takes the form of a renunciation of the
1129: world and a turning inward.   Only those who are trapped in the game
1130: of human morality attempt to 'save' others.  Non-movement, a negative
1131: 'morality', is left in place of the denial of material concerns. 
1132: Vivekananda puts it this way: <br>
1133: <br>
1134: 	“Charity is great, but
1135: the moment you say it is all, you run the risk of running into
1136: materialism...You Christians, have you found nothing else in the
1137: Bible than working for fellow creatures, building hospitals?...The
1138: architect of the universe is going to be taught by the carpenters! 
1139: He has left the world a dirty hole, and you are going to make it a
1140: beautiful place!  That sort of practical religion is good, not bad;
1141: but it is just kindergarten religion. It leads nowhere.”  <br>
1142: <br>
1143: 	“To
1144: have faith one must remain passive vis-a-vis the world.  The believer
1145: must not do anything.”  Cioran<br>
1146: <br>
1147: 	“I know hereby what
1148: advantage belongs to doing nothing (with a purpose). There are few in
1149: the world who attain to the teaching without words, and the advantage
1150: arising from non-action...Heaven and earth do not act from (the
1151: impulse of) any wish to be benevolent; they deal with all things as
1152: the dogs of grass are dealt with. The sages do not act from (any wish
1153: to be) benevolent; they deal with the people as the dogs of grass are
1154: dealt with.”  Tao Te Ching<br>
1155: <br>
1156:  	And Luther maybe puts</div>
1157: <div> it
1158: most damningly, paraphrasing the influential figure of 'Jesus': “How
1159: far different from this is the instruction of Christ: that, we should
1160: rather despise the whole world!”<br>
1161: 	<br>
1162: 	St. Seraphim Rose is
1163: emphatically against any sort of 'utopian' thinking or 'doing good':
1164: “First and foremost I radically question the emphasis upon “action”
1165: itself, upon “projects” and “planning,” upon concern with the
1166: “social” and what man can do about it—all of which acts to the
1167: detriment of acceptance of the given, of what God gives us at this
1168: moment, as well as of allowing His will to be done, not ours.” He
1169: reiterates contrary to human morality: “...it lies, not in the area
1170: of “political commitments” and “social responsibilities...If,
1171: in so doing, we help to ameliorate or abolish a social evil, that is
1172: a good thing—but that is not our goal.”<br>
1173: <br>
1174: 	“The
1175: philosophical portion denounces all work however good, and all
1176: pleasure, as loving and kissing wife, husband or children, as
1177: useless. According to this doctrine all good works and pleasures are
1178: nothing but foolishness and in their very nature impermanent. "All
1179: this must come to an end sometime, so end it now; it is vain.” 
1180: Vivekananda<br>
1181: <br>
1182: 	“...it proceeds to 'disvalue' together with
1183: the self the tribe to which the person belongs, and indeed, together
1184: with that, all existence in general.”  Otto<br>
1185: <br>
1186: 	“Heaven
1187: and earth do not act from (the impulse of) any wish to be benevolent;
1188: they deal with all things as the dogs of grass are dealt with. The
1189: sages do not act from (any wish to be) benevolent; they deal with the
1190: people as the dogs of grass are dealt with.”  Taoism<br>
1191: <br>
1192: 	“The
1193: slime of personal and emotional love is remotely similar to the water
1194: of the Godhead's spiritual being, but of inferior and of insufficient
1195: quantity...human beings can do something to mitigate the horrors of
1196: their situation by 'keeping one another wet with their slime.'  But
1197: there can be no happiness or safety in time and no deliverance into
1198: eternity, until they give up thinking that slime is enough and, by
1199: abandoning themselves to what is in fact their element, call back the
1200: eternal waters...the modern idolaters of progress...prefer an
1201: agonizing and impossible existence on dry land to love, joy and peace
1202: in our native ocean.”  Huxley<br>
1203: <br>
1204: 	“what should i do? work
1205: for a social and political system, make a girl miserable? hunt for
1206: weaknesses in philosophical systems, fight for moral and esthetic
1207: ideals?  it's all too little.” Cioran<br>
1208: <br>
1209: 	“No one has
1210: power over the body or over the world. That means there is nothing we
1211: can do: let the world exist for itself as it pleases or as it thinks
1212: best; we shall learn, and teach others, to do without the world and
1213: without the body that belongs to this world.” Shestov<br>
1214: <br>
1215: 	“All
1216: these experimented virtues make a brilliant showing; for an instant
1217: they are enchanting like an oriental poem: such self-control, such
1218: firmness, such ataraxia, etc., border almost on the fabulous. Yes,
1219: they do to be sure; and also at the bottom of it all there is
1220: nothing.”  Kierkegaard<br>
1221: <br>
1222: 	The positive outlook is
1223: pathetic.  'Positivity' is giving up, a fleeing from Nihilism.  The
1224: Pathetic person wants to say that everything is alright, that every
1225: thing is “just fine”.  It's to ignore our condition.  In other
1226: words, it's to ignore existence.  It is the truly lazy mentality,
1227: which the non-movement of the Nihilist is so often accused of.  The
1228: Nihilist does not like what she sees around her.  She shuts down,
1229: since there is no ultimate difference in her actions.  There is no
1230: such thing as “progress”, only mere change from one state of
1231: affairs to the next; the definition of arbitrary.  The Pathetic
1232: person is trying to hold on to their secret.    The Pathetic person,
1233: instead of retreating into despair pretends to conquer existence by
1234: never fully facing it; most of the time running away from it as fast
1235: as their distractions will take them. <br>
1236: 	The Pathetic person has
1237: surrendered, yet continues to pretend, to go through the motions, day
1238: after day, with that forced smile upon their face.  What kind of
1239: existence is worse than this?  What a sham!  The person who acts out
1240: in the world, promotes “progress”, actually helps others, etc.,
1241: is still nothing more, regardless of all the kicking and screaming,
1242: than the frozen figure of “positivity”.  There is nothing but
1243: fake, inauthentic movements that simulate a life. There is nothing
1244: but insignificant change in an insignificant life.  Progressive
1245: movements are nonsensical.<br>
1246: 	The negative appropriation of
1247: Nihilism simply exemplifies that the distractions of life have not
1248: broken down for such a person; the stranglehold of illusion that
1249: worldly meaning has is cancerous to the Transcendental.  Worldly
1250: meaning spreads into and infects all aspects of our existence,
1251: allowing even the most mundane and ridiculous actions to be
1252: experienced as 'meaningful'.  Assuming an intrinsic value to their
1253: being, the finite, nonsensical, ignorant existence of the human is
1254: put forth as an ideal with what seems as a complete disregard for the
1255: horrific consequences of such a perspective.  The words of Kempus
1256: have still not traveled far enough: “He is the truly wise man, who
1257: counteth all earthly things as dung...”.  Ligotti makes this point
1258: in his unrelentingly pessimistic tone when he declares: “As a
1259: threat to human continuance, nihilism is as dead as God.”  A
1260: participation with, rather than a fleeing from, is necessary to bring
1261: out Nihilism's uttermost forms.  Why would anyone want to
1262: 'participate' in Nihilism?<br>
1263: 	<br>
1264: 	“I am wrong in saying
1265: ‘security,’ for there is no security in this life; understand
1266: that in such<br>
1267: cases I always imply: ‘If they do not cease to
1268: continue as they have begun.’ What misery to live in this world! We
1269: are like men whose enemies are at the door, who must not lay aside
1270: their arms, even while sleeping or eating, and are always in dread
1271: lest the foe should enter the fortress by some breach in the walls. 
1272: How canst Thou wish us to prize such a wretched existence?” 
1273: Theresa of Avila<br>
1274: <br>
1275: 	 “The general acceptance of a doctrine
1276: that denies meaning and value to the world as a whole, while
1277: assigning them in a supreme degree to certain arbitrarily selected
1278: parts of the totality, can only have evil and disastrous results...We
1279: have thought of ourselves as members of supremely meaningful and
1280: valuable communities – deified nations, divine classes and what not
1281: – existing within a meaningless universe.”  Huxley<br>
1282: <br>
1283: 	Instead,
1284: the unreality of the world is pushed forth in a form of either an
1285: ironically morbid 'optimism' or a blindfolded indifference.  One sees
1286: this everyday as he confronts another unthinking robot who projects a
1287: labored smile as he speaks through his teeth about 'how great it is
1288: to be alive.”  Or the restrained shudder of inconvenience that
1289: comes from one who has just heard the news that a relative or friend
1290: has died. <br>
1291: <br>
1292: 	“only the sick man is delighted by life and
1293: praises it so that he won't collapse.” Cioran<br>
1294: <br>
1295: 	"Once
1296: a fishwife was a guest in the house of a gardener who raised flowers.
1297: She came there with her empty basket, after selling fish in the
1298: market, and was asked to sleep in a room where flowers were kept.
1299: But, because of the fragrance of the flowers, she couldn't get to
1300: sleep for a long time. Her hostess saw her condition and said,
1301: 'Hello! Why are you tossing from side to side so restlessly?' The
1302: fishwife said: 'I don't know, friend. Perhaps the smell of the
1303: flowers has been disturbing my sleep. Can you give me my fish-basket?
1304: Perhaps that will put me to sleep'."  So with us. The majority
1305: of mankind delights in this fish smell — this world, this enjoyment
1306: of the senses, this money and wealth and chattel and wife and
1307: children. All this nonsense of the world — this fishy smell — has
1308: grown upon us. We can hear nothing beyond it, can see nothing beyond
1309: it; nothing goes beyond it. This is the whole universe.” 
1310: Vivekananda<br>
1311: <br>
1312: 	“The soulish-bodily synthesis in every man
1313: is planned with a view to being spirit, such is the building; but the
1314: man prefers to dwell in the cellar, that is, in the determinants of
1315: sensuousness. And not only does he prefer to dwell in the cellar; no,
1316: he loves that to such a degree that he becomes furious if anyone
1317: would propose to him to occupy the bel etage which stands empty at
1318: his disposition- for in fact he is dwelling in his own house.” 
1319: Kierkegaard<br>
1320: <br>
1321: 	“And this corporeal element, my friend, is
1322: heavy and weighty and earthy, and is that element of sight by which a
1323: soul is depressed and dragged down again into the visible world...”
1324:  Plato<br>
1325: <br>
1326: 	“...each pleasure and pain is a sort of nail
1327: which nails and rivets the soul to the body.” Plato	<br>
1328: <br>
1329: 	“They
1330: shall wear elegant and ornamented robes, carry a sharp sword at their
1331: girdle, pamper themselves in eating and drinking, and have a
1332: superabundance of property and wealth;— such (princes) may be
1333: called robbers and boasters. This is contrary to the Tao surely!” 
1334: Taoism<br>
1335: <br>
1336: 	The illusory, subjective nature of all value
1337: judgments that pushes us forward, damning us to constantly make value
1338: judgments within a valueless world, with our inability to refrain
1339: from valuations, is Absurd.  As 'solutions' to the Absurd, there are
1340: some who put forth a sense of irony, as suggested by Nagel, while
1341: others assert various forms of 'indifference'.  And while these may
1342: be the closest conception of 'equanimity' that one can seemingly
1343: grasp in the face of Nihilism, this is still only a process of
1344: reasoning about Nihilism, and therefore will be drowned in the
1345: encounter with Nihilism.  Irony or indifference are effectual to
1346: those who are still only 'intellectualizing', rather than actualizing
1347: Nihilism.  An 'attitude' cannot be reached through a process of one
1348: inference to another.  The suggestions of irony or indifference not
1349: only fail as a 'response' due to lending itself as a distraction from
1350: any 'message' that may be found within Nihilism, if there is one to
1351: be found, but it</div>
1352: <div> is
1353: as derisory as requesting the healing of a bullet wound by means of
1354: deduction. 	<br>
1355: <br>
1356: 	“Humanist stoicism is possible for certain
1357: individuals for a certain time: until, that is, the full implications
1358: of the denial of immortality strike home. The Liberal lives in a
1359: fool's paradise which must collapse before the truth of things. If
1360: death is, as the Liberal and Nihilist both believe, the extinction of
1361: the individual, then this world and everything in it-love, goodness,
1362: sanctity, everything-are as nothing, nothing man may do is of any
1363: ultimate consequence and the full horror of life is hidden from man
1364: only by the strength of their will to deceive themselves...” 
1365: Seraphim<br>
1366: <br>
1367: 	“The absurd man thus catches sight of a
1368: burning and frigid, transparent and limited universe in which nothing
1369: is possible but everything is given, and beyond which all is collapse
1370: and nothingness.  He can then decide to accept such a universe and
1371: draw from it his strength, his refusal to hope, and the unyielding
1372: evidence of a life without consolation.”  Camus<br>
1373: <br>
1374: 	The
1375: majority of unthinking humans retreat into a superficial-selfish
1376: interpretation of the religious languages of their time, into the
1377: proximity of their family relations, into the distractions of their
1378: worldly pleasures.  Or as Kierkegaard puts it: “The sectarians
1379: deafen one another with their noise and clamor, keep anxiety away
1380: with their screeching.”  Heidegger says that within the noise and
1381: hustle of the world, by avoiding a confrontation with the Authentic
1382: Self, the 'They' are 'rewarded' with the illusion of 'ascending' and
1383: 'living concretely'.  <br>
1384: <br>
1385: 	“We fear that if theism is
1386: dismissed, only a bottomless pit remains...Many forms of religion are
1387: little more than cultural manifestations of the fear of nothingness. 
1388: That is why people become hysterical when theism is challenged.” 
1389: Spong<br>
1390: <br>
1391: 	“for them there is only one kind of agony, the
1392: one immediately preceding the fall into absolute nothingness.  only
1393: such moments of agony bring about important existential revelations
1394: in consciousness. that is why they expect everything from the end
1395: instead of trying to grasp the meaning of a slow revelatory agony.
1396: the end will reveal too little, and they will die as ignorant as they
1397: have lived.”  Cioran<br>
1398: <br>
1399: 	Nihilism is wholly-destructive to
1400: the world.  The one who experiences Nihilism cannot retreat back into
1401: the world of 'things'.  'Things' are all too transparent.  The world
1402: is dead.  I wish to quote Tønnessen once more at length, due to both
1403: the unfortunately relative obscurity of his paper and for the clear
1404: example in which he describes the human situation:<br>
1405: 	<br>
1406: 	“The
1407: world is what it seems to be to a dry, unimaginative, down to earth,
1408: square-headed stuffshirt about mid-morning after a good night's rest.
1409:  And as for such questions as what it means to live and die –
1410: there's nothing to it, it is commonplace, almost everybody does it. 
1411: We are thrown into an absurdly indifferent world of sticks and stones
1412: and stars and emptiness.  Our “situation” is that of a man who
1413: falls out of the empire state building.  Any attempt at “justifying”
1414: our brief, accelerating fall, the inconceivably short interlude
1415: between our breath-taking realization of our “situation” and our
1416: inexorable total destruction, is bound to be equally ludicrous; i.e.
1417: whether we choose to say: (a) “This is actually quite comfortable
1418: as long as it lasts, let's make the best of it.” or (b) “Let us
1419: at least do something useful while we can,” and we start counting
1420: the windows on the building.  In any event, both attitudes presuppose
1421: an ability to divert ourselves from realizing our desperate
1422: “situation,” to abstract, as it were, every single moment of the
1423: “fall” out of its irreparable totality, to cut our lives up into
1424: small portions with petty, short time-span goals.”  <br>
1425: 	<br>
1426: 	“It
1427: is infinitely comic that a man can understand the whole truth about
1428: how wretched and petty this world is, etc. -that he can understand
1429: this, and then cannot recognize again what he understood; for almost
1430: in the same moment he himself goes off and takes part in the same
1431: pettiness and wretchedness, takes glory in it and receives glory from
1432: it, that is, accepts it.”  Kierkegaard<br>
1433: <br>
1434: 	“We cannot
1435: hide a carrion with roses; it is impossible. It would not avail long;
1436: for soon the roses would fade, and the carrion would be worse than
1437: ever before. So with our lives. We may try to cover our old and
1438: festering sores with cloth of gold, but there comes a day when the
1439: cloth of gold is removed, and the sore in all its ugliness is
1440: revealed.”  Vivekananda<br>
1441: <br>
1442: 	A relevant example of this sort
1443: of 'unthinking', or lack of realization of Transcendence, can be
1444: found within the mindset of a majority of practicing scientists
1445: today.  Science, assuming some form of 'realism', as describing
1446: 'reality' with physical laws, allows one to predict and 'know' what
1447: is or will happen within the world.  However, to stop at this formal
1448: description of reality is unphilosophical.  The Philosopher wants to
1449: ask why the descriptions of science are the way they are.  It is
1450: 'understood' thatd 'particles' and 'molecules' bump into one another
1451: in a particular pattern which produce the physical objects that we
1452: see around us, including ourselves.  To comprehend a description of
1453: things is not to comprehend the 'Why?', i.e. what are the motives
1454: that drive seemingly mindless particles and molecules in acting in
1455: the ways in which they do?  Why are there 'things', why are these
1456: 'things' following a particular rational structure, and why is a
1457: there a 'building' process found within these 'living things'?  These
1458: philosophical questions are shunned by a majority of mainstream
1459: scientific 'intellectuals' who are satisfied with the 'How?'.  This
1460: sort of 'unthinking' is what has produced the 'optimistic' scientism
1461: of the current times.  <br>
1462: <br>
1463: 	“A "scientific"
1464: interpretation of the world as you understand it might consequently
1465: still be one of the stupidest, that is to say, the most destitute of
1466: significance, of all possible world-interpretations:-I say this in
1467: confidence to my friends the Mechanists, who today like to hobnob
1468: with philosophers, and absolutely believe that mechanics is the
1469: teaching of the first and last laws upon which, as upon a
1470: ground-floor, all existence must be built. But an essentially
1471: mechanical world would be an essentially meaningless world! Supposing
1472: we valued the worth of a music with reference to how much it could be
1473: counted, calculated, or formulated -how absurd such a "
1474: scientific " estimate of music would be! What would one have
1475: apprehended, understood, or discerned in it! Nothing, absolutely
1476: nothing of what is really "music" in it!” 
1477: Nietzsche<br>
1478: <br>
1479: 	“One would think that neuroscientists and
1480: geneticists would have as much reason to head for the cliffs because
1481: little by little they have been finding that much of our thought and
1482: behavior is attributable to neural wiring and heredity rather than to
1483: personal control over the individuals we are, or think we are. But
1484: they do not feel suicide to be mandatory just because their
1485: laboratory experiments are informing them that human nature may be
1486: nothing but puppet nature. Not the slightest tingle of uncanniness or
1487: horror runs up and down their spines, only the thrill of discovery.”
1488:  Ligotti<br>
1489: <br>
1490: 	“Generally speaking, science has dulled
1491: people's minds by diminishing their metaphysical consciousness.” 
1492: Cioran<br>
1493: <br>
1494: 	“If I had only to learn how an apple falls to
1495: the ground, or how an electric current shakes my nerves, I would
1496: commit suicide.”  Swami Vivekananda<br>
1497: <br>
1498: 	“To pursue
1499: science, scientists must be justified by something that, strictly
1500: speaking, is not science itself, i.e. curiosity, wonder, faith in
1501: science, the will to master all knowledge, belief that <br>
1502: it will
1503: benefit the world, belief in pure knowledge for its own sake, or some
1504: other breach of objectivity. Because science, apparently, cannot
1505: consistently justify itself, something else must if it is to exist at
1506: all.”  Heisman<br>
1507: 	<br>
1508: 	“In respect to science, which
1509: concerns us especially here in the university, the situation of the
1510: last few decades, a situation which remains unchanged today despite
1511: some cleansing, is easy to see.  Although two seemingly different
1512: conceptions of science are now seemingly struggling against each
1513: other—science as technical and practical professional knowledge and
1514: science as a cultural value in itself—nevertheless both are moving
1515: along the same decadent path of a misinterpretation and disempowering
1516: of the spirit.  In all its areas, science today is a technical,
1517: practical matter of gaining information and communicating it. No
1518: awakening of the spirit at all can proceed from it as science.
1519: Science itself needs such an awakening.”  Heidegger<br>
1520: <br>
1521: 	“The
1522: only question is whether we are willing to fall victim to this cheap
1523: look of things and thus take the whole matter as settled, or whether
1524: we are capable of experiencing a provocative happening in this recoil
1525: of the why-question back upon itself.”  Heidegger<br>
1526: <br>
1527: 	There
1528: is a similar criticism of 'unthinking' to be made against traditional
1529: 'religious' interpretations of the human condition.  Many religious
1530: interpretations of the human experience are replete with theological
1531: assumptions that are not necessarily grounded in human experience;
1532: these take the forms of rituals, prayers, moral declarations,
1533: metaphysical claims, and so on.  However, a language that prepares
1534: 'answers' or 'rational defenses' rather than opening up the mystery
1535: of the Ground of all things must be labeled as a lower form of
1536: idolatry.  <br>
1537: 	These assumptions, as with many religious
1538: languages, are the reason why Heidegger proclaimed that a 'Christian
1539: philosophy' was the equivalent of a 'round-square'.  Heidegger's
1540: thought is that the religious languages that have so far been
1541: developed are restrictive of the human experience in that they are
1542: attempts to go beyond said experience; any descriptions of the Other
1543: is wrongheaded.</div>
1544: <div>  In
1545: other words, religions and their subsequent theologies contain too
1546: many assumptions and 'answers' that shun the mystery of existence. 
1547: Philosophy, on the other hand, as the relentless 'Why?', is what is
1548: needed in order to open up the mystery of Being, instead of closing
1549: it off and pushing it into a closet.  One cannot turn the Other into
1550: the worldly, whether that attempt is through reason or myth.  And
1551: while we are all idolaters, there are simpler, lower forms of
1552: idolatry, since they are not confrontations with Nihilism.     <br>
1553: <br>
1554: 	“So
1555: the despairing self is constantly building nothing but castles in the
1556: air, it fights only in the air. All these experimented virtues make a
1557: brilliant showing; for an instant they are enchanting like an
1558: oriental poem: such self-control, such firmness, such ataraxia, etc.,
1559: border almost on the fabulous. Yes, they do to be sure; and also at
1560: the bottom of it all there is nothing. The self wants to enjoy the
1561: entire satisfaction of making itself into itself, of developing
1562: itself, of being itself; it wants to have the honor of this poetical,
1563: this masterly plan according to which it has understood itself. And
1564: yet in the last resort it is a riddle how it understands itself; just
1565: at the instant when it seems to be nearest to having the fabric
1566: finished it can arbitrarily resolve the whole thing into nothing.” 
1567: Kierkegaard<br>
1568: <br>
1569: 	“...philosophies and doctrines say almost
1570: nothing about death.  the only valid attitude is absolute silence or
1571: a cry of despair.  some people maintain that the fear of death does
1572: not have a deeper justification, because as long as there is an i
1573: there is no death, and once dead there<br>
1574: is no i any longer...what
1575: comfort does this artificial distinction between the i and death
1576: offer a man who has a strong premonition of death?  what meaning can
1577: logical argument or subtle thought have for someone deeply imbued
1578: with a feeling of the irrevocable? all attempts to bring existential
1579: questions onto a logical plane are null and void.  philosophers are
1580: too proud to confess their fear of death and too<br>
1581: supercilious to
1582: acknowledge the spiritual fecundity of illness. their reflections on
1583: death exhibit<br>
1584: a hypocritical serenity; in fact, they tremble
1585: with fear more than anyone else. one should not forget that
1586: philosophy is the art of masking inner torments.”  Cioran<br>
1587: <br>
1588: 	The
1589: lack of questioning concerning the Ground of all things, as witnessed
1590: within both the scientific community and religious community is, yet,
1591: also found (maybe most astonishingly) in the philosophical community.
1592:  The lack of the experience of Nihilism, the 'intellectualizing' and
1593: worldly perspective of most philosophers is obvious when one hears
1594: discussions on The Moral Argument for God's existence, Pascal's
1595: Wager, and the Allegory of the Cave, just to name a few instances.  
1596: <br>
1597: 	Right away, in the first of these examples, it should be
1598: obvious that this is not to say that one must agree with the
1599: soundness of such an argument.  Rather, it is that many philosophers
1600: do not grasp the ontology, or the ground, that such an argument is
1601: attempting to bring forth. The ontology of values is shown to be
1602: misunderstood when there is an epistemological response of: “I can
1603: be a good person without God”.  Knowing the Good, if there were
1604: such a thing, is different from the metaphysical grounding of the
1605: Good; to not be able to make a distinction between epistemology and
1606: ontology is another example of unthinking.  <br>
1607: <br>
1608: 	“No matter
1609: how educated you are, if you don't think intensely about death, you
1610: are a mere fool.  A great scholar – if he is nothing but that –
1611: is inferior to an illiterate peasant haunted by final questions.” 
1612: Cioran<br>
1613: <br>
1614: 	“...he who hasn’t experienced a full
1615: depression alone and over a long period of time— he is a child.” 
1616: Ligotti quoting Jens Bjørneboe<br>
1617: <br>
1618: 	“One who never thinks
1619: of the hour of his death cannot make really spiritual decisions
1620: during his life. He will never be anything more than a short-sighted
1621: opportunist whose decisions will have no lasting value.” 
1622: Merton<br>
1623: <br>
1624: 	If an observer hypothesizes death then, from that
1625: perspective, the observer has no vested interests in life and thus
1626: possible grounds for the most objective view. The more an observer is
1627: reduced to nothing, the more the observer is no longer a factor, the
1628: more the observer might set the conditions for the most rigorous
1629: objectivity...It is likely that most people will not even consider
1630: the veracity of this correlation between death and objectivity even
1631: if they understand it intellectually because most will consciously or
1632: unconsciously choose to place the interests of self-preservation over
1633: the interests of objectivity. In other words, to even consider the
1634: validity of this view assumes that one is willing and able to even
1635: consider prioritizing objectivity over one’s own self-preservation.
1636: Since it not safe to simply assume this on an individual level, let
1637: alone a social level, relatively few are willing and able to
1638: seriously address this issue (and majority consensus can be expected
1639: to dismiss the issue). In short, for most people, including most
1640: “scientists”, overcoming self-preservation is not ultimately a
1641: subject for rational debate and objective discussion.” 
1642: Heisman<br>
1643: <br>
1644: 	The nightmare of existence that Pascal describes
1645: so vividly, with his unrelenting claims of the utter futility of
1646: reason, as he prefaces his 'wager' is inevitably turned into some
1647: strange sort of math equation concerning probabilities.  I believe a
1648: quote, at length, from Pascal is not only worthy, but necessary,
1649: since it reiterates this point directly, and also lays the groundwork
1650: for the need for a further development of a Nihilistic
1651: language...<br>
1652: <br>
1653: “And if besides this he is easy and content,
1654: professes to be so, and indeed boasts of it; if it is this state
1655: itself which is the subject of his joy and vanity, I have no words to
1656: describe so silly a creature. How can people hold these opinions?
1657: What joy can we find in the expectation of nothing but hopeless
1658: misery? What reason for boasting that we are in impenetrable
1659: darkness? And how can it happen that the following argument occurs to
1660: a reasonable man? "I know not who put me into the world, nor
1661: what the world is, nor what I myself am. I am in terrible ignorance
1662: of everything. I know not what my body is, nor my senses, nor my
1663: soul, not even that part of me which thinks what I say, which
1664: reflects on all and on itself, and knows itself no more than the
1665: rest. I see those frightful spaces of the universe which surround me,
1666: and I find myself tied to one corner of this vast expanse, without
1667: knowing why I am put in this place rather than in another, nor why
1668: the short time which is given me to live is assigned to me at this
1669: point rather than at another of the whole eternity which was before
1670: me or which shall come after me. I see nothing but infinites on all
1671: sides, which surround me as an atom, and as a shadow which endures
1672: only for an instant and returns no more. All I know is that I must
