1673: soon die, but what I know least is this very death which I cannot
1674: escape...Such is my state, full of weakness and uncertainty. And from
1675: all this I conclude that I ought to spend all the days of my life
1676: without caring to inquire into what must happen to me. Perhaps I
1677: might find some solution to my doubts, but I will not take the
1678: trouble, nor take a step to seek it; and after treating with scorn
1679: those who are concerned with this care, I will go without foresight
1680: and without fear to try the great event, and let myself be led
1681: carelessly to death, uncertain of the eternity of my future state."
1682: Who would desire to have for a friend a man who talks in this
1683: fashion?”  <br>
1684: 	<br>
1685: 	This is Pascal's Wager.  The
1686: 'intellectuals' who mutate Pascal's insights into the human condition
1687: into a strange math problem concerning probabilities misses the
1688: entire environment in which the wager is put forth.  This is not a
1689: casual decision made from acute calculations of 'finite losses' and
1690: 'infinite gains'.  That is propaganda language on Pascal's part.  As
1691: you can see from his own words above, all finitudes fall under the
1692: category of 'lost'.  There are no winners here.  One cannot find what
1693: one is looking for in this nightmarish existence. This is Pascal's
1694: Wager.<br>
1695: 	Another example is Plato's Allegory of the Cave when it
1696: is taken as no more than 'myth' that has no direct correlation to
1697: reality and seen with no more 'validity' than any another story
1698: contained within a 'religious' text.  For instance, with reference to
1699: Plato's Cave, the same can be said about roller-coasters.  Imagine
1700: there is a builder who constructs a roller-coaster.  The builder,
1701: however, never actually steps foot on the ride.  If one was to ask
1702: the builder what his roller-coaster was like, what an experience of
1703: riding the roller-coaster was like, any attempt to explain the
1704: experience, regardless of the builder's knowledge of every dip, turn,
1705: curve, and flip, would lack any particular substance.  <br>
1706: 	This is
1707: the exact situation most philosophers are in when they speak of
1708: Plato's Cave as 'only' a 'myth', while up at their 'prestigious'
1709: lecterns.  Their lack of direct experience turns any words spewed out
1710: to be covered in ignorance at best, and dogmatism at worst.   This is
1711: an empirical example of how the worldly, materialistic thinking has
1712: affected even the ones who are supposed to be the 'questioners of
1713: truth' on its deepest levels<br>
1714: 	As we will see when we come to our
1715: fuller discussion of mysticism and Entheogenic experiences, the cave
1716: allegory, as a transcendent reality, is not the type of thing that is
1717: 'believed in' or to be taken as a mere metaphor, rather it is
1718: something to be experienced first hand.  When used in the proper set
1719: (environment) and setting (proper psychological preparation for
1720: participation in the experience of the Other) an uncanny world opens
1721: up that leaves any naturalistic interpretation feeling inadequate, if
1722: not utterly wrongheaded.</div>
1723: <div> 
1724: Without making claims about the mystical-type experience 'proving' a
1725: Transcendent reality, there is a correlation between the experience
1726: and the knower that may be useful for our purposes.   <br>
1727: 	This
1728: sentiment may be able to be made more clear with a mentioning of
1729: 'uncanniness'.  The feeling that one is 'at home' here in the world
1730: is one that is the most common view of human experience. The casual
1731: nature in which humans scurry from one project to the next, one
1732: pursuit of 'happiness' after another, is the way the world operates
1733: on the whole.  The question that a philosopher might ask is something
1734: along these lines: what is there to be 'casual' about with regards to
1735: flying around on a rock at 40,000 miles an hour, around a fireball
1736: 'about' to explode causing utter annihilation, in the 'middle' of a
1737: possibly endless void, in a possible infinitude of space and time? 
1738: <br>
1739: 	That there are 'miles per hour', that there are physical
1740: 'things', that one has hands to type, made of what we refer to as
1741: 'flesh', and on and on...these are all assumptions taken as 'normal'.
1742:  That you are reading these words and that they are impregnating your
1743: brain immediately as each word, one after the other, is read.  Put
1744: simply, there is nothing to be casual about.   Zapffe finds this
1745: 'casualness' counter to the fundamental human condition. He writes:
1746: “Such a ‘feeling of cosmic panic’ is pivotal to every human
1747: mind.”  Conversely, a fleeing from such panic, without any
1748: questioning of a deeper “Why?', is also 'pivotal' to every human
1749: mind.<br>
1750: 	In the life-story of Buddha, one hears Kierkegaard's
1751: echo: "Oh worldly men! how fatally deluded! beholding everywhere
1752: the body brought to dust, yet everywhere the more carelessly living;
1753: the heart is neither lifeless wood nor stone, and yet it thinks not
1754: 'all is vanishing!'" Then turning, he directed his chariot to go
1755: back, and no longer waste his time in wandering. How could he, whilst
1756: in fear of instant death, go wandering here and there with lightened
1757: heart!”<br>
1758: <br>
1759: 	“Certain facts of which too keen a perception
1760: would act detrimentally to the life-force are, for most men,
1761: impossible of realization: i.e. , the uncertainty of life, the decay
1762: of the body, the vanity of all things under the sun. When we are in
1763: good health, we all feel very real, solid, and permanent; and this is
1764: of all our illusions the most ridiculous, and also the most obviously
1765: useful from the point of view of the efficiency and preservation of
1766: the race.”  Underhill<br>
1767: 	<br>
1768: 	To overcome would be to be a
1769: god.  Nietzsche's Uberman, Kierkegaard's Knight of Faith, Heidegger's
1770: Equanimity, Vivekananda's wholly unselfish being, all are ideals that
1771: cannot be reached, at least in the form of which human beings
1772: presently exist.  The mystics also attempt to claim a
1773: 'reconciliation', but this is only done in the possibility of
1774: forgetting the human situation in their fleeting moments of ecstasy. 
1775: Regardless of the validity of mystical-type experiences, which do
1776: seem to hold as a foundational element of human experience, these
1777: experiences are drowned in the experience of Nihilism. 
1778: Meaninglessness subsumes mysticism.  Tillich expresses this precisely
1779: as an aspect of his philosophy: “The experience of meaninglessness
1780: is more radical than mysticism.  Therefore it transcends the mystical
1781: experience.”  <br>
1782: 	Tillich, with all his comprehensive words on
1783: Nihilism, is still working within his brand of Christianity.  This
1784: undue emphasis on worldly 'courage', both by himself and his readers,
1785: comes off, at best, as suspect.  Is Tillich's worldly conception of
1786: 'courage' only to be held by those who have only either
1787: 'intellectualized' Nihilism or who are attempting to flee it? 
1788: Tillich's own words suggest something is being lost in translation
1789: when he describes the role of meaninglessness as fundamental to the
1790: 'meaning of life': <br>
1791: <br>
1792: 	“The answer must accept, as its
1793: precondition, the state of meaninglessness.  It is not an answer if
1794: it demands the removal of this state; for that is just what cannot be
1795: done.  He who is in the grip of doubt and meaninglessness cannot
1796: liberate himself from this grip but he asks for an answer which is
1797: valid within and not outside the situation of despair. He asks for
1798: the ultimate foundation of what we have called the 'courage of
1799: despair.'  There is only one possible answer, if one does not try to
1800: escape the question; namely that the acceptance of despair is in
1801: itself faith.  In this situation the meaning of life is reduced to
1802: despair about the meaning of life.”<br>
1803: 	<br>
1804: 	How does one
1805: square an emphasis on 'courage' with the rest of Tillich's philosophy
1806: that is built, one could argue entirely, upon despair and
1807: meaninglessness, as anything more than a conjuring illusion for
1808: everyday people?  Does Tillich believe his own words?  Is Tillich
1809: preaching a 'happy' message for the masses, instead of a 'truth' that
1810: he in the end holds to?  As he was preaching 'courage', did he forget
1811: the Protestant King's words?  “God forbid! Even if God were to
1812: offer me paradise in order that I might last forty more years in this
1813: life, I wouldn't want it.  I'd rather hire a hangman to knock my head
1814: off.  That's how bad the world is now.  It's full of nothing but
1815: devils, so that one can't with anything better than a blessed end and
1816: to get away.  Nor do I bother with physicians.”  <br>
1817: 	And how
1818: about Vivekananda, who Tillich shares a great part of his philosophy
1819: with?  “I hate this world, this dream, this horrible nightmare with
1820: its churches and chicaneries, its books and blackguardisms, its fair
1821: faces and false hearts, its howling righteousness on the surface and
1822: utter hollowness beneath, and, above all, its sanctified
1823: shopkeeping.”  Does Tillich actually believe that this 'courage' of
1824: facing the necessary idolatrous nature of concrete religious symbols
1825: takes priority over suicide?  Is this just a facade or another
1826: example of fleeing from Nihilism?  Does he truly believe in it?  Or,
1827: even if all are touched by the Nihilistic experience, however
1828: lightly, is he preaching to the masses who are mostly ignorantly
1829: fleeing from the experience of Nothingness?<br>
1830: <br>
1831: 	“See how we
1832: are flying like hunted hares from all that is terrible, and like
1833: them, hiding our heads and thinking we are safe. See how the whole
1834: world is flying from everything terrible.” Vivekananda<br>
1835: <br>
1836: 	Ultimate
1837: skepticism and doubt are built in to the syntax of Nihilism, even in
1838: the instances of those few who have experienced something mystical
1839: and wish to interpret their experience as Transcendent.  This
1840: interpretation of Nihilism is essentially locked into the human
1841: situation which is, at least in part, naturalistic.  Even if one is
1842: let out of this 'prison-house' for a short period of recreation, one
1843: must be thrown back into the cell of the Nothing of the world; this
1844: is until death allows for a full escape into the Nothingness.  As
1845: Pascal realized: “scepticism helps religion.”<br>
1846: <br>
1847: 	“If
1848: you believe in God, you are mad without having gone mad.” 
1849: Cioran<br>
1850: <br>
1851: 	“Those who believe that they believe in God, but
1852: without any passion in their heart, without anguish of mind, without
1853: uncertainty, without doubt, without an element of despair even in
1854: their consolation, believe only in the God-Idea, not in God Himself.”
1855:  Unamuno <br>
1856: <br>
1857: 	'Who wants God?  That is the question.  Do you
1858: think the mass of people in the world want God and cannot get him? 
1859: That cannot be...A disciple went to his master and said to him, "Sir,
1860: I want religion." The master looked at the young man, and did
1861: not speak, but only smiled. The young man came every day, and
1862: insisted that he wanted religion. But the old man knew better than
1863: the young man. One day, when it was very hot, he asked the young man
1864: to go to the river with him and take a plunge. The young man plunged
1865: in, and the old man followed him and held the young man down under
1866: the water by force. After the young man had struggled for a while, he
1867: let him go and asked him what he wanted most while he was under the
1868: water. "A breath of air", the disciple answered. "Do
1869: you want God in that way? If you do, you will get Him in a moment,"
1870: said the master. Until you have that thirst, that desire, you cannot
1871: get religion, however you may struggle with your intellect, or your
1872: books, or your forms. Until that thirst is awakened in you, you are
1873: no better than any atheist; only the atheist is sincere, and you are
1874: not.”  Vivekananda<hr>
1875: <h2><em>Madness, Nonexistence, and The Other</em>&nbsp;</h2><h3>-The Suicide of the Renouncer-</h3><br>“Our
1876: ultimate concern can destroy us as it can heal us.”  Tillich<br>
1877: <br>
1878: “It
1879: is perfectly possible that a person with 'existential frustration,'
1880: 'ontological despair,' or simply 'sub-clinical depression' may,
1881: because of his abnormal position, be in a better position to look
1882: through the camouflage of life that still is deceiving the 'healthy'
1883: psychotherapists.”  Tønnessen <br>
1884: <br>
1885: “It may be that in a
1886: certain sense, i.e., "practically speaking," men are right:
1887: but they have not the strength to reduce madness and death to
1888: silence. Madness and death can be driven away temporarily, but they
1889: will return, and, having returned, will carry out their purpose: they
1890: will ask man questions which he would rather forget forever.” 
1891: Shestov<br>
1892: <br>
1893: I believe that those who <br>
1894: speculate that a
1895: full apprehension of man’s condition would drive him insane are
1896: right, quite literally right.” Becker<br>
1897: <br>
1898: “Human
1899: cowardice, as Kierkegaard said more than once, cannot endure what
1900: madness and death have to tell us.” Shestov<br>
1901: <br>
1902: “A man who
1903: has become conscious of the absurd is forever bound to it.” 
1904: Camus<br>
1905: <br>
1906: “The situation becomes farcical only when the
1907: hobby-man attempts to “unsick” the lifetime devoted philosopher,
1908: to cure him, as it were, of being insalubriously pessimistic!” 
1909: Herman Tonseman <br>
1910: <br>
1911: “In life, man proposes, God disposes.”
1912:  Huxley (Kempus)<br>
1913: <br>
1914: “Now more than ever, we should build
1915: monasteries...for those who believe in everything and for those who
1916: believe in nothing.  Where to escape?  There no longer</div>
1917: <div>
1918: exists a single place where we can professionally execrate this
1919: world.” Cioran<br>
1920: <br>
1921: “Thus I, a healthy and a happy man, was
1922: brought to feel that I could live no longer, that an irresistible
1923: force was dragging me down into the grave.”  Tolstoy<br>
1924: <br>
1925: “We
1926: have invented happiness.”  Nietzsche <br>
1927: <br>
1928: “Men are so
1929: necessarily mad, that not to be mad would amount to another form of
1930: madness.”  Pascal<br>
1931: <br>
1932: “...the ascetic ideal has meant so
1933: much to man, lies expressed the fundamental feature of man's will,
1934: his horror vacui [horror of a vacuum]: he needs a goal - and he will
1935: sooner will nothingness than not will at all. - Am I not understood?
1936: - Have I not been understood? - "Certainly not, sir?" -
1937: Well, let us begin at the beginning.”  Nietzsche <br>
1938: 		<br>
1939: 	“Thinking
1940: of God and not religion, of ecstasy and not mysticism.  The
1941: difference between the theoretician of faith and the believer is as
1942: great as between the psychiatrist and the psychotic.”  These words
1943: by Cioran lay out the mindset of this entire work.  The rejection of
1944: the worldly is one that is necessary for these words to have any
1945: resonance, yet it is the most difficult position to comprehend; the
1946: experience of Nothing is so rare.   <br>
1947: <br>
1948: 	“...under the
1949: ascendancy of falling and publicness, ‘real’ anxiety is rare.” 
1950: Heidegger<br>
1951: <br>
1952: 	Nihilism must be properly distinguished from
1953: mental illness, more specifically from being represented in some form
1954: of worldliness.  Although there is much more that needs to be said
1955: regarding this issue, the sentiment can at least be acknowledged in
1956: the words of Becker: “Full humanness means full fear and trembling,
1957: at least some of the waking day...When you get a person to look at
1958: the sun as it bakes down on the daily carnage taking place on earth,
1959: the ridiculous accidents, the utter fragility of life, the
1960: powerlessness of those he thought most powerful—what comfort can
1961: you give him from a psychotherapeutic point of view?”  St. Seraphim
1962: Rose expresses the distinction this way: “Nihilism is, most
1963: profoundly, a spiritual disorder, and it can be overcome only by
1964: spiritual means; and there has been no attempt whatever in the
1965: contemporary world to apply such means.” <br>
1966: 	Mitchell Heisman
1967: also damns any attempt at relating psychology or 'religion' to
1968: Nihilism.  Nihilism expresses the fundamental, 'incurable' ground of
1969: the human condition:<br>
1970: <br>
1971: 	“Can the meaninglessness of
1972: existence be cured with therapy?  Lie therapy, whether religious or
1973: secular, is overwhelmingly the normative state of the human race. 
1974: Put another way, there may be sound psychological reasons why radical
1975: nihilism is not a condition prescribed by therapists.  Yet if
1976: psychologists are scientists, and their own methodology confirms the
1977: scientific view of human beings as material things in motion, on what
1978: grounds can they criticize nihilism in itself?  Only insofar as
1979: psychologists are not scientists; insofar as they are non-objective
1980: partisans of the ‘life party’, psychologists fundamentally are
1981: irrevocably biased against consideration of my point of view.
1982: Psychological explanations can be used, not only to evade the
1983: nihilistic, but to evade the scientific equation of the larger
1984: material world with a material view of one’s own mind — and thus
1985: to misunderstand the problem.”  <br>
1986: 	<br>
1987: 	How does madness and
1988: suicide relate to Nihilism?  Are madness and suicide necessary
1989: consequences of Nihilism?  If  madness is related to Nihilism, is it
1990: to be welcomed, or even pursued?  Is  there a difference between a
1991: worldly suicide and a suicide resulting from Nihilism?  Is there a
1992: difference of a particular 'irrational' neurosis and the terror that
1993: comes from the insights of an experience of Nihilism? <br>
1994: 	There is
1995: one well-documented case of suicide due to Nihilism in the writing of
1996: Mitchell Heisman who lived out Nihilism. seemingly, in its lack of
1997: Transcendence.  Heisman asks: “Can one live a philosophy of the
1998: nihilistic, reconciling meaninglessness with every thought and
1999: emotion at every moment? If active unbelief were the highest
2000: organizing principle of a life, would the consequence be rational
2001: self-destruction? Could suicide represent the pinnacle of the
2002: rational life realized?”  His suicide suggests the impossibility of
2003: such an existence; or rather the utter rationality of suicide in a
2004: non-rational world. <br>
2005: 	In his two-thousand page suicide note,
2006: Heisman expresses a propulsion towards a new language of Nihilism
2007: that though he may have been seeking after, he could not find.  He
2008: describes his ambitions accordingly: “The implications of life’s
2009: meaninglessness have not been elicited with sufficient ruthlessness.
2010: My methodology is honesty to the point of absurdity; honesty without
2011: mercy; honesty unprejudiced by morals, aesthetics, faith, or hope.
2012: When all illusions have been dispelled, at the end of overcoming
2013: subjectivities, biases, and prejudices towards life, one encounters
2014: the possibility of rational negation of self-interest; rational
2015: self-annihilation; rational self-destruction. The experiment in
2016: nihilism is to seek out precisely those truths that are most deadly
2017: and destructive to me. To will death through truth and truth through
2018: death.”  ('possibility' of rational self-destruction?  Ha!  Isn't
2019: that  adorable?)<br>
2020: 	Underhill, a hundred-years earlier, describes
2021: the seemingly necessary nature of an undertaking such that Heisman
2022: pursued: “But the true intellectualist, who concedes nothing to
2023: instinct or emotion, is obliged in the end to adopt some form of
2024: sceptical philosophy. The horrors of nihilism, in fact, can only be
2025: escaped by the exercise of faith...”  All the same, Heisman lacked
2026: such a applicable language of 'faith'.  Is there anything behind this
2027: thought that drives two thinkers, as seemingly as diverse as Heisman
2028: and Underhill, that has been forgotten amongst the busyness of the
2029: world, which suggests something fundamental about the human
2030: condition?  <br>
2031: 	Moreover, there is the issue of whether
2032: 'something' is missing in Heisman's interpretation of Nihilism or
2033: whether Underhill is 'guilty' of falling pray to the fear of an
2034: uncompromising Nihilism.  One is driven to  'faith' ('faith' here is
2035: used in relation to the Nothing of the world, 'beyond good and evil',
2036: beyond a human morality.  One of the most accurate definitions of
2037: 'faith' in relation to Nothingness comes from Kierkegaard: “...the
2038: opposite of sin is not virtue but faith.”  'Faith' is the
2039: experience of something 'beyond' the Nothingness of the world) and
2040: the other to suicide.  Does one of them one of have a fuller
2041: conception of Nihilism?  Is one of them 'right'?  <br>
2042: 	Throughout
2043: Heisman's expansive suicide note one finds it replete with many other
2044: sentiments that have been put forth by both 'saints' and other
2045: 'religiously' minded people, although stated in a wholly naturalistic
2046: interpretation of a Nihilism that lacks any notion of Transcendence. 
2047: Heisman speaks of denying the self, the wretchedness of human life,
2048: the contradictory nature of human emotions, the nothing of the world,
2049: a possible transcendental aspect of human consciousness (which he
2050: found in the music of Bach), yet the saints lived out their lives
2051: under an umbrella of a religious language (regardless of how porous
2052: the umbrella), while Heisman was drowned in the naturalistic language
2053: of the 21st century.  Nihilism is all-powerful in its experience,
2054: regardless of the human limitations of interpretation.  <br>
2055: 	And
2056: even though he expresses, at least in part, some desire to move past
2057: a strictly naturalist viewpoint, he was unable to do so.  The
2058: following quote from Heisman shows at least a hint for the
2059: possibility of a language that moves Nihilism past a
2060: wholly-naturalistic view, while not entirely escaping its grasp. 
2061: Worth mentioning is that we shouldn't take what Heisman has to say
2062: here in its simplest form, which is tempting to do with the words
2063: that he has chosen (reducing his 'discovery' down to an 'evolutionary
2064: basis), i.e. the common criticism of 'God' as arising from some
2065: pragmatic function which stems from a place of 'desperation', as a
2066: need for an 'escape' from the human condition. This would be to miss
2067: the deeper interpretation of grounding 'God' within and essentially
2068: as Nihilism, rather than as an answer derived for comfort or as some
2069: sort of 'conclusion to the problem'.<br>
2070: <br>
2071: 	“When all choices
2072: are equal, equality is compatible with total randomness. If all
2073: choices are equal, then the choice of death is equal to the choice of
2074: life. If life is meaningless, then God is nothing.  But a funny thing
2075: happened on the way to nihilistic self-destruction. It was precisely
2076: through radical disbelief that I discovered an evolutionary basis for
2077: God.  Most secular people in the West are simply not ruthless enough
2078: in their nihilism to vivisect belief to death. Yet vivisecting belief
2079: to death exposes how the original monotheistic conception of God
2080: likely arose out of ruthless realism. Monotheism may have originated
2081: out of a skeptical, nihilistic, materialistic objectivity that
2082: annihilated the biologically based subjectivity of the self, and thus
2083: created something ‘out of nothing’.”<br>
2084: <br>
2085: 	Now, this is
2086: not to say that Heisman was necessarily 'wrong' in his action of
2087: suicide, that he should have been saved, or that even a Nihilistic
2088: language that I am envisioning could have stopped his suicide.  The
2089: question is: is suicide the answer?  Would a language that
2090: encompassed and embraced the kindred words of Heisman and the saints
2091: allow for Heisman to live as a saint? (Does one even want to live as
2092: a saint?)  Conversely, would a Nihilistic
2093: language/symbolism/method/etc. entail suicide?<br>
2094: <br>
2095: 	The
2096: Suicide is not one to be judged in any particular manner.  There is a
2097: question of how to think about the suicide of the businessman who
2098: just lost his job or the person who has just been broken up with by
2099: the 'love of their life' and the Nihilist engulfed in Nothingness. 
2100: Is there such a thing as an authentic suicide?  Is the suicide of the
2101: one who has lost</div>
2102: <div> a
2103: (all) 'worldly' distraction(s) that they relate their 'entire' selves
2104: to the same as a Heisman who has already discovered the Nothing of
2105: the world?  The person who is a slave to the world seemingly has a
2106: slight 'opening up' of Nihilism with the destruction of what they
2107: hold as meaningful, with the loss of a job or a relationship, for
2108: instance, yet they seem to flee into suicide as a relief.  (I don't
2109: like this last sentence)<br>
2110: 	But what about the one who has
2111: experienced Nihilism and knows that the material positions, even the
2112: 'person to person' connections/interactions are inevitably the
2113: iquevalent to the walking dead?  These worldly groundings have
2114: already died for the Nihilist.  Do we stop the worldly suicide and
2115: not the Nihilistic suicide?  If one has not experienced the death of
2116: the world, then can that person be dragged back into the world,
2117: talked off of the ledge?  Conversely, if the world has died for a
2118: person, and everything within the world is insignificant (Heidegger,
2119: obviously), then what is there to drag such a person back to?  Is the
2120: development of a Nihilistic language of transcendence even 'worth'
2121: the effort?  <br>
2122: 	Is the Nihilistic suicide not the 'optimal' set
2123: of movements?  Is the Nihilistic suicide authentic in the sense that
2124: it is not a 'fleeing' or escaping from the world, but rather a
2125: movement that is the inevitable decision to release oneself into the
2126: Divine?  The strange aversion to one following the 'logic of suicide'
2127: is almost as universal as the unquestioned following of the 'logic of
2128: life'. <br>
2129: <br>
2130: 	“When a human being takes his life in
2131: depression, this is a natural death of spiritual causes. The modern
2132: barbarity of ‘saving’ the suicidal is based on a hair-raising
2133: misapprehension of the nature of existence.”  Zapffe<br>
2134: <br>
2135: 	The
2136: issue is way more complex than this black and white illustration. 
2137: The 'jumping' suicides of 9/11 are one such example of the complexity
2138: of 'suicide'; there is a possibility that these are not even
2139: 'suicides' in any 'real' sense.  With that said, there does seem to
2140: be something missing from the common suicide that is not lacking in
2141: Heisman's.  This 'something' seems to be that the common suicide is
2142: worldly and concerned with the ego, rather than the transcendent-self
2143: (although one could forcefully argue that Heisman was driven by his
2144: ego). This distinction of suicides, and the mindsets and motivations,
2145: can be interpreted in a similar way via Kierkegaard- <br>
2146: <br>
2147: 	“It
2148: is almost as though the Christian must be puffed up because of this
2149: proud elevation above everything men commonly call misfortune, above
2150: that which men commonly call the greatest evil. But then in turn
2151: Christianity has discovered an evil which man as such does not know
2152: of; this misery is the sickness unto death. What the natural man
2153: considers horrible -- when he has in this wise enumerated everything
2154: and knows nothing more he can mention, this for the Christian is like
2155: a jest. Such is the relation between the natural man and the
2156: Christian; it is like the relation between a child and a man: what
2157: the child shudders at, the man regards as nothing. The child does not
2158: know what the dreadful is; this the man knows, and he shudders at it.
2159: The child's imperfection consists, first of all, in not knowing what
2160: the dreadful is; and then again, as an implication of this, in
2161: shuddering at that which is not dreadful. And so it is also with the
2162: natural man, he is ignorant of what the dreadful truly is, yet he is
2163: not thereby exempted from shuddering; no, he shudders at that which
2164: is not the dreadful: he does not know the true God, but this is not
2165: the whole of it, he worships an idol as God.”<br>
2166: 	<br>
2167: 	The one
2168: who sees the Nothing of the world, loses interest with it.  This or
2169: that activity is received as a distraction from this Other that seems
2170: to be nagging at their conscience.  When one experiences the
2171: meaninglessness of the world, this is the experience that one wants
2172: to surround oneself with.  The patchwork is no longer going to work. 
2173: Some people are trying to carrying buckets of water from a river to a
2174: campground.  Some people's buckets have one hole, another has two
2175: holes, another's completely dilapidated.  The one who has the least
2176: holes, is able to get through life, is able to hold onto life, onto
2177: the water.  This is how most people seem to get through life, utterly
2178: blind of their condition, i.e. the chasm of death whose opening
2179: awaits every step, thought, breath.  The person who has a few more
2180: holes in his bucket is grasping to hold on, trying ever so hard to
2181: get at least some of the water to the campground, yet he may not make
2182: it.  The last example seems to stand no chance.  Is there an
2183: authenticity in the final person 'giving up'?  (I don't know if I
2184: like this, I'll have to reread).<br>
2185: 	There are a lot of people like
2186: this who are struggling to keep themselves distracted, to keep the
2187: water in their bucket, sort to speak, with their work, wealth,
2188: family, hobbies, etc.  They feel a sense of dissatisfaction, yet they
2189: keep plugging along in what they halfway recognize as a necessary
2190: failure.  All the water, from all the buckets, will eventually be
2191: lost.  Then there are the people with Swiss cheese buckets.  These
2192: people cannot be held up by any of life's distractions anymore. 
2193: Everything has slipped through their fingers.  All meaning has left
2194: the temporal realm.  <br>
2195: <br>
2196: 	“When death is the greatest
2197: danger, one hopes for life; but when one becomes acquainted with an
2198: even more dreadful danger, one hopes for death. So when the danger is
2199: so great that death has become one's hope, despair is the
2200: disconsolateness of not being able to die.”  Kierkegaard<br>
2201: <br>
2202: “i want to die but I am sorry that I want to die.  this is the
2203: feeling experienced by those who abandon themselves to nothingness.”
2204:  Cioran  <br>
2205: <br>
2206: “He points to those who 'do not want to live
2207: and do not know how to die.'”  Tillich quoting Seneca<br>
2208: 	<br>
2209: 	There
2210: is a dichotomy between the logic of life and the logic of suicide. 
2211: One will always speak past the other.  There is no communicating the
2212: logic of suicide to one who has not even questioned, never mind
2213: experienced the Nothing of existence; the experience of Nothingness
2214: is not within their frame of reference.  The vast majority of the
2215: human species follows the logic of life.  They feel at home here in
2216: the world; there is nothing to worry about here.  The one who has
2217: been brought to Nothingness has no way of 'arguing' away the
2218: 'meaning' put onto the lives of those within the world. 
2219: 	<br>
2220: <br>
2221: 	“Psychiatry even works on the assumption that the
2222: ‘healthy’ and viable is at one with the highest in personal
2223: terms. Depression, ‘fear of life,’ refusal of nourishment and so
2224: on are invariably taken as signs of a pathological state and treated
2225: thereafter. Often, however, such phenomena are messages from a
2226: deeper, more immediate sense of life, bitter fruits of a geniality of
2227: thought or feeling at the root of antibiological tendencies. It is
2228: not the soul being sick, but its protection failing, or else being
2229: rejected because it is experienced – correctly – as a betrayal of
2230: ego’s highest potential.”  Zapffe<br>
2231: <br>
2232: 	Is there even any
2233: point to ask why others are so uncomfortable with thinking?! Reason
2234: leads you to skepticism.  Skepticism brings you to despair.  Despair
2235: drives you to Nihilism.  Nihilism opens up the Other.  The Other is
2236: terrifyingly strange.  My misery seems unbearable at best.  What is
2237: this feeling of meaninglessness, if not Divine?  What could possibly
2238: cause this experience?  The fear of madness is one hindrance along
2239: the path to the Other; how demanding 'thinking' can be.  Wouldn't to
2240: be stolen by madness be the realization of an ideal?  Or why not the
2241: 'never-ending' adventure into madness?  <br>
2242: 	<br>
2243: 	“Humanly
2244: speaking, he is mad and cannot make himself understandable to anyone.
2245:  And yet 'to be mad' is the mildest expression.  If he is not viewed
2246: in this way, then he is a hypocrite, and the higher he ascends this
2247: path, the more appalling a hypocrite he is.”  Kierkegaard<br>
2248: 	<br>
2249: 	“He
2250: stands outside human concerns and draws close to the divine; ordinary
2251: people think he is disturbed and rebuke him for this, unaware that he
2252: is possessed by god...he would like to fly away, but he cannot; he is
2253: like a bird fluttering and looking upward and careless of the world
2254: below; and he is therefore thought to be mad.”  Plato<br>
2255: <br>
2256: 	St.
2257: Francis de Sales warns of the consequences that come from the world
2258: when one takes on a 'journey' such as Heisman's: “DIRECTLY that
2259: your worldly friends perceive that you aim at leading a devout life,
2260: they will let loose endless shafts of mockery and misrepresentation
2261: upon you; the more malicious will attribute your change to hypocrisy,
2262: designing, or bigotry; they will affirm that the world having looked
2263: coldly upon you, failing its favour you turn to God; while your
2264: friends will make a series of what, from their point of view, are
2265: prudent and charitable remonstrances. They will tell you that you are
2266: growing morbid; that you will lose your worldly credit, and will make
2267: yourself unacceptable to the world; they will prognosticate your
2268: premature old age, the ruin of your material prosperity; they will
2269: tell you that in the world you must live as the world does; that you
2270: can be saved without all this fuss; and much more of the like
2271: nature.”<br>
2272: 	And then, as if to mock them back, he calls out what
2273: he sees as their superficiality or unthinking: “We have all seen
2274: men, and women too, pass the whole night, even several in succession,
2275: playing at chess or cards; and what can be a more dismal, unwholesome
2276: thing than that? But the world has not a word to say against it, and
2277: their friends are nowise troubled. But give up an hour to meditation,
2278: or get up rather earlier than usual to prepare for Holy Communion,
2279: and they will send for the doctor to cure you of hypochondria or
2280: jaundice!”<br>
2281: 	Elsewhere, he puts the disaster of an unthinking
2282: life that ought to resonate with anyone who considers</div>
2283: <div>
2284: themselves 'human': “Again, while you were dancing, many a soul has
2285: passed away amid sharp sufferings; thousands and tens of thousands
2286: were lying all the while on beds of anguish, some perhaps untended,
2287: unconsoled, in fevers, and all manner of painful diseases. Will you
2288: not rouse yourself to a sense of pity for them? At all events,
2289: remember that a day will come when you in your turn will lie on your
2290: bed of sickness, while others dance and make merry.”  
2291: <br>
2292: <br>
2293: 	“Moreover, it is largely customary to mock at the
2294: melancholy; and in good society it is an unwritten law that every one
2295: shall bring a certain quota of contentment and gayety, or else remain
2296: in chambered solitude.”  Saltus<br>
2297: <br>
2298: 	Suicide need not be
2299: necessary even when it is not taken as an abomination.  The
2300: indefinite, 'constructive' nature of the content that is found within
2301: Nihilism is something that is easily 'confused' due to its
2302: unknowability.  One may be brought to suicide, yet it may bring
2303: another to a 'Some-thing'.  <br>
2304: <br>
2305: 	“...how do you fill out an
2306: empty life?  With women, books, or worldly ambitions?  No matter what
2307: you do, the starting point is boredom, and the end self-destruction.”
2308:  Cioran<br>
2309: <br>
2310: 	“To find that everything lacks reality and not
2311: to put an end to it all, this inconsistency is not an inconsistency
2312: at all: taken to extremes, the perception of the void coincides with
2313: the perception of the whole, with the entrance into the All.”
2314: Cioran<hr>
2315: <h2><em>The Startling Encounter with Transcendent Nothingness</em>&nbsp;</h2>
2316: <h3>-Reconnecting with the Remembered Self-</h3>
2317: <br><h3><br></h3><div>"Why does
2318: evolution, as we ascend the ladder of life, foster instead of
2319: diminishing the capacity for useless mental anguish, for long, dull
2320: torment, bitter grief?”  Underhill</div>
2321: <br>
2322: “One simply
2323: lacks any reason for convincing oneself that there is a true world.”
2324:  Nietzsche<br>
2325: <br>
2326: “How is such an experience possible if the
2327: ultimate is that which transcends all possible experience?”
2328: Tillich<br>
2329: <br>
2330: “It grasps the mind with terrifying and
2331: fascinating power.  It breaks into the ordinary reality, shakes it
2332: and drives it beyond itself in an ecstatic way.”  Tillich<br>
2333: <br>
2334: “It
2335: is the emotion of a creature, submerged and overwhelmed by its own
2336: nothingness in contrast to that which is supreme above all
2337: creatures.”  Otto<br>
2338: “This is no doubt why modern life, weary
2339: of positivism and disillusioned by it, but possessed of neither the
2340: strength nor the desire to go beyond the boundaries it has
2341: established, has made such a fierce attack upon the work of the
2342: mystics.”  Shestov<br>
2343: <br>
2344: “The initial revelation of any
2345: monastery: everything is nothing.  Thus begin all mysticisms.  It is
2346: less than one step from nothing to God, for God is the positive
2347: expression of nothingness.”  Cioran<br>
2348: <br>
2349: “The last step
2350: towards nihilism is the disappearance into divinity.” 
2351: Cioran<br>
2352: <br>
2353: “This is a practical philosophy that needs to be
2354: applied experientially to be known.”  Swami Rama<br>
2355: <br>
2356: 	Is
2357: there some form of empirical data that coincides with the suggestion
2358: that the Nihilistic experience has both a Naturalistic and a
2359: Transcendent component?  How, then, is Nihilism with its
2360: Transcendental interpretation to be, in any substantial sense,
2361: distinguished from Naturalism?  Is Nihilism necessarily to be reduced
2362: to naturalism?  Are there characteristics of Nihilism that separates
2363: itself from, and go beyond, naturalism?  Is there any way to 'touch'
2364: the Transcendent aspect of Nihilism or are we stuck in the situation
2365: of naturalism that Underhill summarizes so succinctly: “It says in
2366: effect: The room in which we find ourselves is fairly comfortable.
2367: Draw the curtains, for the night is dark: and let us devote ourselves
2368: to describing the furniture.”  Is this our reality?<br>
2369: 	Even with
2370: all the strong descriptions of Nihilism that are found within a
2371: Tillich, a Cioran, a Heisman, a Kierkegaard, and so forth, there
2372: seems to be something missing.  Was Nietzsche right when he stated
2373: that there was no reason to convince oneself of a 'true world'? 
2374: Would, or should, Heisman have blown his brains out all over the
2375: Harvard library steps if he encountered something more within
2376: Nihilism?  Maybe.  Maybe not.  <br>
2377: 	(maybe re-word this first
2378: sentence. sounds a little too 'academic'/analytic tradition)  We
2379: claim the Nihilistic experience is in part 'mystical'.  The
2380: groundwork for a wholly Transcendent experience is laid within in the
2381: experience of the Nothing of the world which allows for the further
2382: possibility of an 'opening up' of the Other.  The experience of
2383: Nothingness within (and of) the world does not bring forth any
2384: 'concrete' forms of the Other.  However, an inexplicable, haunting,
2385: sensation lingers within, creating a suspicion, intensifying to the
2386: point of a “pushing”, for one to confront the Other by a
2387: 'clearing of the path', a renunciation of the distractions of the
2388: world, a pursuit and participation with the Nothingness.<br>
2389: <br>
2390: 	“since
2391: i will not die right away, nor regain my innocence, going through the
2392: same routine motions every day is sheer madness.  banality must be
2393: overcome at all costs and the way cleared for transfiguration.  how
2394: sad to see men bypass themselves, neglect their own destiny instead
2395: of rekindling the light they carry within them or getting drunk on
2396: their abysmal darkness!”   Cioran	<br>
2397: <br>
2398: 	“If we truly want
2399: to follow God we must seek to be other-worldly.”  Tozer<br>
2400: <br>
2401: 	What
2402: is lacking with only the Naturalistic interpretation of Nihilism is
2403: the 'fuller' experience.  The descriptions found in even the most
2404: 'profound thinkers' is what we may refer to as the 'diminished'
2405: (Naturalistic) versions of Nihilism, i.e. the Nothing of the world. 
2406: There is another aspect of the Nihilistic experience that is
2407: seemingly 'beyond', but that is not to say incoherent with, or
2408: totally separate from, the diminished form of Nihilism.  Regardless
2409: of the acendant nature of the 'other half'(?) of the experience, this
2410: form is even still subsumed by the Nothing of the world.  In other
2411: words, the 'deeper' experiences of Nihilism that may or may not be
2412: entirely worldly are as rarely explored or experienced as its
2413: Naturalistic counterpart.  With that said, there is a 'wider'
2414: experience of Nihilism, there is a more direct confrontation to be
2415: had.  This is found in what is normally referred to as the
2416: psychedelic experience.  <br>
2417: 	The psychedelic experience can be
2418: mystical.  What is a 'mystical experience'?  Broadly stated, the
2419: mystical experience eradicates the ego.  The worldly 'I' is erased
2420: from its entirety, yet there is something that remains.  There is a
2421: disconnect from the worldly flesh-body that is experienced as wholly
2422: incorporeal.  While there is no ego to be found, this 'something'
2423: that resembles a 'you' remains; yet this different sort of 'you' is
2424: not all there is either.  There is a 'bigger something'; there is a
2425: meeting with.  <br>
2426: 	Nihilism in its fully Transcendent forms is
2427: experienced in such a way that it can be described as an Augmented
2428: version of experiencing the Nothing of the world.  The Nothing of the
2429: world and the Augmented experiences of Nihilism are simply variants
2430: of each other. The Augmented form of Nihilism coincides with what is
2431: normally labeled as 'mystical'.  This Augmented form of Nihilism is a
2432: true 'opening up' of the Transcendent.  There is close similarity in
2433: the comparison to the 'two-sided' experiences of Nihilism, as
2434: Augmented and as Naturalistic, as is found within human consciousness
2435: as the divided-self as part Transcendent and part Natural.  The
2436: 'external' appearance of Augmented Nihilism is one that is indeed
2437: taken as an encounter with the Other, while the naturalistic aspect
2438: of Nihilism is one that 'hears a 'message' that is coming 'from me
2439: and beyond me', yet within the world.  And although they differ in
2440: pure experience, they are not in conflict with one another; they lend
2441: themselves to each other in a cohesive fashion.    <br>
2442: <br>
2443: 	“It
2444: is a mistake to supposed that mysticism derives from a softening of
2445: the instincts, from a compromised vitality...To get a sense of them,
2446: imagine a Hernando Cortez in the middle of an invisible geography. 
2447: The German mystics were conquerors too.”  Cioran<br>
2448: <br>
2449: 	"Quietism":
2450: the difference between the tense stillness of the athlete and the
2451: limp passivity of the sluggard, who is really lazy, though he looks
2452: resigned. True "Quiet" is a means, not an end: is actively
2453: embraced, not passively endured. It is a phase in the self's growth
2454: in contemplation; a bridge which leads from its old and uncoordinated
2455: life of activity to its new unified life of deep action--the real
2456: "mystic life" of man.”  Underhill<br>
2457: <br>
2458: 	“He calls
2459: suffering the “gymnastic of eternity,” the “terrible initiative
2460: caress of God”; recognizing in it a quality for which the
2461: disagreeable rearrangement of nerve molecules cannot account.
2462: Sometimes, in the excess of his optimism, he puts to the test of
2463: practice this theory with all its implications. Refusing to be
2464: deluded by the pleasures of the sense world, he accepts instead of
2465: avoiding pain, and becomes an ascetic; a puzzling type for the
2466: convinced naturalist, who, falling back upon contempt—that
2467: favourite resource of the frustrated reason—can only regard him as
2468: diseased.”  Underhill<br>
2469: <br>
2470: 	“Believe me, children, one who
2471: would know much about these high matters would often have to keep his
2472: bed, for his bodily frame could not support it.”  Tauler<br>
2473: 	<br>
2474: 	The
2475: Augmented aspect of the Nihilistic experience stays mostly hidden due
2476: to a lack of participation or pursued confrontation with the Other. 
2477: Many don't even know that it exists; or if they do, they have
2478: misconceptions about it due to the babble from people who have never
2479: experienced it themselves.  Most 'spiritual' journeys consists of
2480: meditation, contemplation, various mortifications, and so on.  Yet,
2481: even though these practices are performed with the aim of attaining a
2482: mystical experience, most fail at ever achieving this end (at least
2483: in such an awe-inspiring fashion).  <br>
2484: <br>
2485: 	“In order to go
2486: into it, man must empty himself of all the finite contents of his
2487: ordinary life; he must</div>
2488: <div>
2489: surrender all preliminary concerns for the sake of the ultimate
2490: concern.  He must transcend the division of existence...The ultimate
2491: is beyond this division, and he who wants to reach the ultimate must
2492: overcome this division in himself by meditation, contemplation and
2493: ecstasy.” Tillich<br>
2494: <br>
2495: 	“First Bach fugues, a bore...But
2496: training changes the structure of our spiritual experiences.  In due
2497: course, contact with an obscurely beautiful poem, an elaborate piece
2498: of counterpoint or of mathematical reasoning, causes us to feel
2499: direct intuitions of beauty and significance.”  Huxley<br>
2500: <br>
2501: 	These
2502: traditional practices need not be necessarily ridiculed as either
2503: inefficient nor as hindrances; these practices are seemingly
2504: beneficial.  But is there something more?  Is there another way that
2505: 'guarantees'  a confrontation with the Other?  We believe there is a
2506: way and that it does not take years of study, ritual, and practice in
2507: order to achieve this state (although these practices cannot be
2508: ignored as they are useful tools for the experience, even if they do
2509: not, in themselves, produce an encounter).  The psychedelic journey
2510: is one that, if properly prepared for, can open up the abyss of
2511: existence in which no words, thoughts, systems, religions, and so
2512: forth, can prepare one for.  <br>
2513: 	If one one wants to pursue, if
2514: one wants to confront and participate with the Other, with Augmented
2515: Nihilism, then one only needs a few grams of psylocibin for their
2516: worldview to be irreconcilably disrupted.  For those who have
2517: experienced this Mystical Nothingness, there is an unthinking
2518: enthusiasm involved which pushes one into a frenzy of confusion due
2519: to the uncanny and terrifying nature that is like nothing found
2520: within the world. <br>
2521: 	 As with the 'diminished' forms of Nihilism,
2522: there is a strong motivation to move past the Augmented Nihilism in
2523: an attempt to cover up the horrid experience of Nothingness, throwing
2524: Nihilism into some form of worldliness in order to act in the world. 
2525: This 'moving past' is the birth of all confusion and discord.   When
2526: one grasps and misuses finite language as a literal description of
2527: the Wholly-Other the experience of Nihilism is inevitably obfuscated.
2528:  One must speak to oneself when discussing the Other.  Finite human
2529: language is necessarily symbolic in nature with regards to speaking
2530: of the Other. <br>
2531: <br>
2532: 	     “...any experience, no matter how
2533: spiritual, could only yield us an It.” Buber<br>
2534: <br>
2535: 	These
2536: words are only a distraction, worthless nothings which are only
2537: written out of cowardice to confront the Other.  I begged to be
2538: 'kept' where I was as I experienced a timeless, Ultimate unity; I
2539: felt 'at home' and infinitely satisfied.  With that said, it has been
2540: over a year and I have not been able to push myself into another
2541: encounter; terrifying bliss like nothing I have ever experienced
2542: before.  I feel both unworthy of 'Its' presence and I also cannot
2543: escape the pathetic, naturalistic side of my divided-self from being
2544: paralyzed with fear from another encounter.  <br>
2545: 	What if one does
2546: 'meet' this Other and yet is still not convinced?  What if one meets
2547: this Other and still does not know what to do?  The real question is
2548: this: what if one 'meets' this Other and still cannot hold to any
2549: delusion set forth by other humans who have supposedly gone through
2550: this experience, nor develop any symbols that show relevance to the
2551: Other? All concrete symbols of Transcendence are dead.  <br>
2552: 	What
2553: do we say about this unspeakable world?  Is it purely illusory?  A
2554: misinterpretation of a trick that is played on us by our mind?  For
2555: one who has not experienced, this may pass off as an 'explanation'. 
2556: For those who have experienced the Augmented forms of Nihilism, it
2557: cannot be dismissed so easily.  There is a something that one
2558: encounters within this Augmented experience of Nihilism.  One may
2559: rush to call it 'God'. Others may dismiss it as the workings of the
2560: 'subconscious'.  Whatever the explanation, it is peculiar that the
2561: Nothingness found within the world is magnified to unmeasurable
2562: heights when in the face of this Other, this 'something'.  This Other
2563: world is experienced as more real than the mundane, everyday world. 
2564: The 'true' illusion becomes what all consider the 'real' or 'only'
2565: world.<br>
2566: <br>
2567: 	“Mescalin opens up the way of Mary, but shuts
2568: the door on that of Martha. It gives access to contemplation— but
2569: to a contemplation that is incompatible with action and even with the
2570: will to action, the very thought of action. In the intervals between
2571: his revelations the mescalin taker is apt to feel that, though in one
2572: way everything is supremely as it should be, in another there is
2573: something wrong. His problem is essentially the same as that which
2574: confronts the quietest...”  Huxley<br>
2575: <br>
2576: 	There is a
2577: terrifying bliss that surrounds your entire Being, without you being
2578: who you are.  There is a dissolution of the ego, the worldly self
2579: that is concerned with security, money, relationships, and so on. 
2580: All human aspects of worldly-consciousness drop out; they do not
2581: simply become insignificant, as with experiencing the Nothing of the
2582: world, rather they utterly dissipate, they are no where to be
2583: found.<br>
2584: <br>
2585: 	“Absence, extinction, and unoccupancy--these are
2586: not the Buddhist conception of emptiness. Buddhists' Emptiness is not
2587: on the plane of relativity. It is Absolute Emptiness transcending all
2588: forms of mutual relationship, of subject and object, birth and death,
2589: God and the world, something and nothing, yes and no, affirmation and
2590: negation. In Buddhist Emptiness there is no time, no space, no
2591: becoming, no-thing-ness; it is what makes all these things possible;
2592: it is a zero full of infinite possibilities, it is a void of
2593: inexhaustible contents.”  Suzuki<br>
2594: <br>
2595: 	To confront, there
2596: must be a dissolution of the 'I' or of the ego; this is what
2597: psylocibin does with utter ease.  There must be renunciation.  The
2598: Other demands that we renounce.  If you are do not reduce yourself
2599: into as pure a form humility as one can achieve, if you do not
2600: renounce the ego, the self, if you hold on, well, then, you will
2601: bring a nightmare upon yourself like never experienced before.  The
2602: preparation of renunciation begins with the experiencing the Nothing
2603: of the world.  One must give up holding onto the deadness of the
2604: world.  The transitory nature of all things within the world are
2605: nothing but distractions that deflect the Other.  What is it to
2606: experience oneself as Nothing, if it is not a dissolution of the ego?
2607:  To renounce worldly desires, ambitions, and any other form of
2608: illusion, is to embrace the Nothing of the world, which allows for a
2609: 'clearer' experience of the Other.  The dissolution of the 'I' brings
2610: forth the greatest sensation of Unity.  A feeling of pure
2611: consciousness that is One with all of existence; it is pure
2612: existence.  <br>
2613: <br>
2614: 	“To the extent that there is attachment to
2615: 'I,' 'Me,' 'Mine,' there is no attachment to, and therefore no
2616: unitive knowledge of, the divine Ground.”  Huxley<br>
2617: <br>
2618: 	“The
2619: ultimate abandonment of one’s role is not to have a self as a fixed
2620: point of reference; it is the freedom to manifest God through one’s
2621: own uniqueness. This monk had hit bottom. But the bottom in the
2622: spiritual journey is also the top. To be no one is to be everyone. To
2623: be no self is to be the true Self. To be nothing is to be everything.
2624: In a sense, it is to be God.”  Keating <br>
2625: <br>
2626: 	“...it would
2627: seem that there is within each of us an enemy which we tolerate at
2628: our peril. Jesus called it "life" and "self," or
2629: as we would say, the self-life. Its chief characteristic is its
2630: possessiveness: the words "gain" and "profit"
2631: suggest this. To allow this enemy to live is in the end to lose
2632: everything.”  Tozer<br>
2633: <br>
2634: 	“The void- myself without me- is
2635: the liquidation of the adventure of the 'I'- it is being without any
2636: trace of being, a blessed engulfment, an incomparable disaster.”
2637: Cioran <br>
2638: <br>
2639: 	“I am sorry sometimes that God no longer fills
2640: us with dread. If only we could feel again the primordial quiver of
2641: dread in front of the unknown!” Cioran<br>
2642: <br>
2643: <br>
2644: 	When is
2645: one truly ready to confront something Infinite, something wholly
2646: Other?  This is not a 'meeting' with another object within the world,
2647: this is a confrontation with Existence itself, in the fullest sense
2648: of the term.  This confrontation is so different that it can easily
2649: push one into a state of panic that will be unrecognizable.  The
2650: naturalistic side of the divided-self, the side that pulls us down
2651: into the world, turns produces a fear that is unimaginable.  The
2652: 'Hellish' confrontation with the Other, the terrifying bliss of the
2653: experience, is something that cannot be boxed away within human
2654: language or dismissed as illusory or 'natural'.   <br>
2655: <br>
2656: 	“Some
2657: people talk as if meeting the gaze of absolute goodness would be fun.
2658: They need to think again.”  C. S. Lewis<br>
2659: 	<br>
2660: 	“...terror
2661: fraught with an inward shuddering such as not even the most menacing
2662: and overpowering created thing can instil.”  Otto<br>
2663: <br>
2664: 	"The
2665: infinite depth repels and horrifies me; the infinite height attracts
2666: and satisfies me.”  Tolstoy<br>
2667: <br>
2668: 	“Although this painful
2669: martyrdom of horrible desolation and passive purgation be so
2670: tremendous, that with reason it hast gotten the name of Hell amongst
2671: mystick Divines, (because it seems impossible to be able to live a
2672: moment with so grievous a torment; so that with great reason it may
2673: be said, that he that suffers it, lives dying, and dying lives a
2674: lingring death) yet know, that it is necessary to endure it, to
2675: arrive at the sweet, joyous and abundant riches of high contemplation
2676: and loving union: and there has been no holy Soul, which has not
2677: passed through this spiritual martyrdom and painful torment.” 
2678: Molinos<br>
2679: <br>
2680: 	“Mysticism revolves around the passion for
2681: ecstasy and a horror of the void.  One cannot know one without the
2682: other...Once it has totally rejected the world, the soul is ripe for
2683: a long-term and fecund emptiness…One sees nothing except
2684: nothingness.  And the latter has become</div>
2685: <div>
2686: everything. Ecstasy is plenitude in a void, a full void.  It is an
2687: overwhelming frisson which convulses nothingness, an invasion of
2688: being in absolute emptiness.”  Cioran<br>
2689: 	<br>
2690: 	Is there a way
2691: to 'guide' oneself through this confrontation?  Music, with its
2692: abstract nature, is suited best for such a journey.  For assistance
2693: with the death of the 'I', to release from the naturalistic side of
2694: the self, the abstract nature of music, with its strange use of
2695: plucking sounds from 'nowhere' with the equally important 'rests' in
2696: between each note, as a form of language, allows one to more-fully
2697: dispense with the natural-self.  Music without words will do best,
2698: since any human voice may cause a distraction and pull one down and
2699: away from the Other.  Bach's organ music, a God-like instrument with
2700: God-like tones, will guide one through the journey with the Other. 
2701: Bach's organ works express fully his meditations on death.  Focus on
2702: the bass/pedal notes, let the highs and mids come at you, and allow
2703: for the Bach, who speaks fluently the 'language of God', to show you
2704: the Other.  <br>
2705: <br>
2706: 	“...and yet it does not matter that he’s
2707: all in bits. The whole is disorganized. But each individual fragment
2708: is in order, is a representative of a Higher Order. The Highest Order
2709: prevails even in the disintegration. The totality is present even in
2710: the broken pieces. More clearly present, perhaps, than in a
2711: completely coherent work. At least you aren’t lulled into a sense
2712: of false security by some merely human, merely fabricated order. You
2713: have to rely on your immediate perception of the ultimate order. So
2714: in a certain sense disintegration may have its advantages. But of
2715: course it’s dangerous, horribly dangerous. Suppose you couldn’t
2716: get back, out of the chaos…”  Huxley<br>
2717: <br>
2718:  	“...the
2719: Schopenhauerian theory in majorem musicae gloriam [for the greater
2720: glory of music] - that is to say, by means of the sovereignty of
2721: music, as Schopenhauer understood it; music abstracted from and
2722: opposed to all the other arts, music as the independent
2723: art-in-itself, not like the other arts, affording reflections of the
2724: phenomenal world, but rather the language of the will itself,
2725: speaking straight out of the "abyss" as its most personal,
2726: original, and direct manifestation.”  Nietzsche<br>
2727: <br>
2728: 	“Bach's
2729: music is the medium of heavenly transfiguration.” 
2730: Cioran<br>
2731: <br>
2732: 	“...Bach often meditated on death...Handel
2733: compared to Bach, is of this world.  Bach is divine...”
2734: Cioran<br>
2735: <br>
2736: 	“Listening to Bach, one sees God come into
2737: being.  His music generates divinity.  After a Bach...one feels that
2738: God must exists.  Otherwise, Bach's music would be only heartrending
2739: illusion.  Theologians and philosophers wasted so many days and
2740: nights searching for proofs of his existence, ignoring the only valid
2741: one: Bach.”  Cioran<br>
2742: <br>
2743: 	“If humans are emotion machines,
2744: then music must work, in some sense, like a machine.<br>
2745: Music could
2746: be a form of emotional technology to control my own behavior
2747: intelligently. In other words, if emotions are the products of
2748: material processes, then art could be viewed as a form of
2749: <br>
2750: technology. From the standpoint of this musical materialism, as
2751: one of the most extreme implications of an unadulterated materialism,
2752: lay a possible solution to dominance of my own <br>
2753: analytic and
2754: objective tendency to materialize everything. I began to listen to
2755: music, especially German music (and especially Wagner and Bach), as a
2756: form of technology to counter my own tendencies to view everything as
2757: material or technology.”  Heisman<br>
2758: <br>
2759: 	“Bach is ground
2760: from outside of myself that makes up for the nihilistic lack of
2761: ground within myself. Bach counters my material self-consistency and
2762: its tendency towards self-decomposition <br>
2763: with a form of
2764: holistic-mind order.”  Heisman<br>
2765: <br>
2766: 	Plato describes
2767: Augmented Nihilism and the seemingly 'true' mindset to have in the
2768: face of this Nothing:<br>
2769: <br>
2770: 	“...the entire soul is pierced
2771: and maddened and pained, and at the recollection of beauty is again
2772: delighted. And from both of them together the soul is oppressed at
2773: the strangeness of her condition, and is in a great strait and
2774: excitement, and in her madness can neither sleep by night nor abide
2775: in her place by day. And wherever she thinks that she will behold the
2776: beautiful one, thither in her desire she runs. And when she has seen
2777: him, and bathed herself in the waters of beauty, her constraint is
2778: loosened, and she is refreshed, and has no more pangs and pains; and
2779: this is the sweetest of all pleasures at the time, and is the reason
2780: why the soul of the lover will never forsake his beautiful one, whom
2781: he esteems above all; he has forgotten mother and brethren and
2782: companions, and he thinks nothing of the neglect and loss of his
2783: property; the rules and proprieties of life, on which he formerly
2784: prided himself, he now despises, and is ready to sleep like a
2785: servant, wherever he is allowed, as near as he can to his desired
2786: one, who is the object of his worship, and the physician who can
2787: alone assuage the greatness of his pain.”<br>
2788: 	<br>
2789: 	At the same
2790: time, two-thousand years ago, Plato describes the divided-self, and
2791: the fleeing into the world of constant distraction, of the human
2792: condition as if he is speaking of one of our contemporaries: “Now
2793: he who is not newly initiated or who has become corrupted, does not
2794: easily rise out of this world to the sight of true beauty in the
2795: other; he looks only at her earthly namesake, and instead of being
2796: awed at the sight of her, he is given over to pleasure, and like a
2797: brutish beast he rushes on to enjoy and beget; he consorts with
2798: wantonness, and is not afraid or ashamed of pursuing pleasure in
2799: violation of nature.”  <br>
2800: <br>
2801: 	As has been said, the Nihilist
2802: cannot turn to the dead world of distractions.  Everything has died. 
2803:  How does one begin to interpret this experience that no words can
2804: grasp?  The world's religions may be of some help, but they can never
2805: settle the issue.  Is there some language that can be developed in
2806: order to help one properly address the Nothing?  Huxley sums up this
2807: need quite well: <br>
2808: <br>
2809: 	“...the ultimate Reality is not
2810: clearly and immediately apprehended, except by those who have made
2811: themselves loving, pure in heart and poor in spirit. This being so,
2812: it is hardly surprising that a theology based upon the experience of
2813: nice, ordinary, unregenerate people should carry so little
2814: conviction.  This kind of empirical theology is on precisely the same
2815: footing as an empirical astronomy, based upon the experience of
2816: naked-eye observers.”   <br>
2817: 	<br>
2818: 	Philosophy, or conceptual
2819: thinking, seems to help pave the way to allow for a better
2820: 'understanding' of the ineffable, but, even still, there is no new
2821: 'knowledge' gained.  The universe will dissipate before your very
2822: eyes.  The flow of 'information' will come at you at an infinite
2823: rate.  You will be demanded to renounce, to let go, to show true
2824: humility.  The non-movement as a result of a total lack of values, of
2825: an utter insignificance of the human world, goes hand in hand with a
2826: Quietist contemplative existence within the Nothing of the world. 
2827: Why is it that the psychedelic experience is one which ushers in a
2828: mystical experience that confirms the meaninglessness of the world?
2829: <br>
2830: <br>
2831: 	“...whilst in that state of abstraction rising higher,
2832: perceiving there is a place beyond any bodily condition, adding still
2833: and persevering further in practising wisdom, rejecting this fourth
2834: dhyâna, firmly resolved to persevere in the search, still contriving
2835: to put away every desire after form, gradually from every pore of the
2836: body there is perceived a feeling of empty release, and in the end
2837: this extends to every solid part, so that the whole is perfected in
2838: an apprehension of emptiness. In brief, perceiving no limits to this
2839: emptiness, there is opened to the view boundless knowledge. Endowed
2840: with inward rest and peace, the idea of 'I' departs, and the object
2841: of 'I'—clearly discriminating the non-existence of matter, this is
2842: the condition of immaterial life.”  Buddha<br>
2843: <br>
2844: 	If one
2845: wishes to rush to a 'conclusion' and attempt to wrap up the infinite
2846: within one of the worldly religions, in order to live in the world,
2847: in order to 'make sense' of such a confrontation, then one is only
2848: fleeing from Nihilism and ultimately doing a disservice to the
2849: 'something' that one meets on the psychedelic journey.  This is not
2850: about belief or developing a system or trying to box in Nihilism. 
2851: One must accept the utter mystery behind Nihilism as something too
2852: 'powerful' to be confined in the finite.  There is only the Infinite
2853: Conclusion.  <br>
2854: 	<br>
2855: 	One encounters Nothingness in the Transcendent experience of Nihilism.   <br>
2856: <br>
2857: 	“Therewith, they
2858: learn that It is the Cause of all things and yet Itself is nothing,
2859: because It super-essentially transcends them all.” 
2860: Pseudo-Dionysius<br>
2861: 	“In statements such as Eckhart's, God is
2862: equated with nothing.”  Huxley<br>
2863: <br>
2864: 	Huxley further goes on
2865: to confirm the Nihilist's experience of the Nothing of oneself:
2866: <br>
2867: 	“Cheap,” I commented. “Trivial. Like things in a
2868: five-and-ten.” And all this shoddiness existed in a closed, cramped
2869: universe. “It’s as though one were below decks in a ship,” I
2870: said. “A five-and-ten-cent ship.” And as I looked, it became very
2871: clear that this five-and-ten-cent ship was in some way connected with
2872: human pretensions, with the portrait of Cézanne, with A.B. among the
2873: Dolomites overacting his favorite character in fiction. This
2874: suffocating interior of a dime-store ship was my own personal self;
2875: these gimcrack mobiles of tin and plastic were my personal
2876: contributions to the universe.”<br>
2877: 	Why is this important?  The
2878: 'truth' of the experience is one that is confirmed by those separated
2879: by time, space, culture, and so on.  A repeating of the words of
2880: Huxley can be found in the mystic St. Molinos: “Knowing that thou
2881: art nothing, that thou canst do nothing, and art worth just nothing,
2882: thou wilt quietly embrace passive drynesses, thou wilt endure
2883: horrible desolations; thou wilt undergo spiritual</div>
2884: <div>
2885: martyrdoms and inward torments. By means of this Nothing thou must
2886: die in thy self, many ways, at all times, and all hours, Keeping thy
2887: self in Nothing, thou wilt bar the door against every thing that is
2888: not God”.   Molinos further states: “But the happy Soul which is
2889: gotten to this holy hatred of it self, lives overwhelmed, drowned and
2890: swallowed up in the depth of its own Nothing.”<br>
2891: 	Within
2892: mystical experiences human truth, better put, human rationality is no
2893: longer a concern.  There are 'things' that are brought forth that no
2894: human language can describe, that no human being can fully
2895: comprehend.  The experience is one that must be dealt with
2896: subjectively. <br>
2897: <br>
2898: 		   “In life, man proposes, God
2899: disposes.”  Huxley<br>
2900: <br>
2901: 	Smith describes his encounter with
2902: the Other as such: “The world into which I was ushered was strange,
2903: weird, uncanny, significant, and terrifying beyond belief.”  Smith,
2904: describing his experience himself in a Transcendental manner, also
2905: declares: “Revelations can be terrifying.”  The mystical
2906: experience is not like anything of this world.<br>
2907: 	Huxley describes
2908: a part of his psychedelic experience as follows: “And then t here
2909: is the horror of infinity...I found myself all at once on the brink
2910: of panic.  This, I suddenly felt, was going too far.  Too far, even
2911: though the going was into intenser beauty, deeper significance.  The
2912: fear, as I analyze it in retrospect, was of being overwhelmed, of
2913: disintegrating under a pressure of reality greater than a mind,
2914: accustomed to living most of the time in a cosy world of symbols,
2915: could possibly bear.  The literature of religious experience abounds
2916: in references to the pains and terrors overwhelming those who have
2917: come, too suddenly, face to face with some manifestation of the
2918: Mysterium tremendum.  In theological language, this fear is due to
2919: the incompatibility between man’s egotism and the divine purity,
2920: between man’s self-aggravated separateness and the infinity of
2921: God.”<br>
2922: <br>
2923: 	“Quite naturally, then, almost no one figures
2924: their time to be ill-spent in bickering about some point of scripture
2925: or a psycho-philosophical poser rather than in sizing up some
2926: superlative individuals who have called into question what we are or
2927: what we might be aside from slaves of our egos.”  Ligotti<br>
2928: 	<br>
2929: 	Is
2930: there any better explanation for why philosophers and theologians
2931: have 'missed the mark' in their sand-trap of pursuing 'rationality'
2932: instead of direct experience?  Is there any better reason one could
2933: give for the seemingly endless stagnation and frustration that ensues
2934: from the 'intellectualizing' of these issues?  Is there any doubt as
2935: to why the dogmas of the naturalist and of the worldly 'religious'
2936: are trapped in a stalemate with their attempts to 'convince' one
2937: another with reason and rationality?  Reason and rationality have
2938: their place, but if there is not an attempt to go beyond, then one
2939: necessarily can only obtain a constrained portion of the human
2940: condition.  <br>
2941: 	Huxley sums it up with a question: “How many
2942: philosophers, how many theologians, how many professional educators
2943: have had the curiosity to open this Door in the Wall? The answer, for
2944: all practical purposes, is, None.”<br>
2945: 	There is no need for a
2946: concern over 'spookiness' in any 'supernatural' or 'new age' sense. 
2947: The particular mystical-type experiences that I have briefly
2948: mentioned are not anything special.  By 'special', I mean that these
2949: experiences can be induced into any one willing to undertake such a
2950: task.  There is no worry of any 'new age' or 'supernatural' powers
2951: underlying any of the claims made here.  Any person in the proper set
2952: (psychological preparation) and setting (proper surroundings and
2953: environment), along with a definite intention for the pursuit is
2954: welcome to 'test' the results of such an experience for themselves. 
2955: There is no need for years of meditation, chanting, yoga, or any
2956: other traditional 'religious' interlocutors; although these methods,
2957: and others, can be used in correlation with psychedelics which may
2958: bring out a fuller and deeper experience.  <br>
2959: 	One is now simply
2960: left to interpret said experience.  Is it wholly naturalistic?  Is
2961: there anything veritical about the psychedelic experience?  Speaking
2962: from personal experience, the experience that one encounters with the
2963: use psychedelics is not only not contradictory with the philosophy of
2964: Nihilism (as laid out in these pages), it rather confirms it quite
2965: strongly.  This does not prove anything.  While one is tempted to
2966: refer to the psychedelic experience as an encounter with the Other,
2967: with all its Transcendent, noetic, and paradoxical qualities, there
2968: is no explicit contradiction in reducing the experience down to a
2969: naturalist interpretation. Regardless, of what one holds about the
2970: Augmented form of Nihilism, we get reduced back to our normal state
2971: of consciousness, left with the Nothing of the world.  St. Theresa
2972: laments: “Oh, what a distress it is for my soul to have to return
2973: to hold commerce with this world after having had its conversation in
2974: heaven!  To have to play a part in the sad farce of this earthly
2975: life!”  <br>
2976: 	“This, however, is the sublime melancholy of our
2977: lot that every You must become an It in our world.  However
2978: exclusively present it may have been in the direct relationship- as
2979: soon as the relationship has run its course or is permeated by means,
2980: the You becomes an object among objects, possibly the noblest one and
2981: yet one of them, assigned its measure and boundary.”  The
2982: actualization of the work involves a loss of actuality. Genuine
2983: contemplation never lasts long; the natural being that only now
2984: revealed itself to me in the mystery of reciprocity has again become
2985: describable, analyzable, classifiable- the point in which manifold
2986: systems of laws intersect.”  Buber<br>
2987: 	“How, in other words,
2988: can one be a saint and still organize scientific movements of
2989: world-historical importance? How does one lean on God and give over
2990: everything to Him and still stand on his own feet as a passionate
2991: human being? These are not rhetorical questions, they are real ones
2992: that go right to the heard of the problem of 'how to be a man'- a
2993: problem that no one can satisfactorily advise anyone else on, as the
2994: wise William James knew. The whole thing is loaded with ambiguity
2995: impossible to resolve.”  Becker<hr><h2><em>The Symbolic Resonance of Nothingness</em></h2><h3>-Subtle, Vague, yet Persistent Echo-</h3><br>“There is a nagging
2996: suspicion that the diagnostics are exploiting Heidegger's exotic
2997: language in order to “keep talking,” and thus keep calm and
2998: unaffected by the horror of the bottomless abyssus humanae
2999: conscientiae with which they are incessantly confronted.”  
3000: Tønnessen<br>
3001: <br>
3002: “What cannot be translated into mystical
3003: language does not deserve to be experienced.”  Cioran<br>
3004: <br>
3005: “Martin
3006: Heidegger is what happens when one believes in one’s own emotions,
3007: i.e. poetry.”  Heisman<br>
3008: <br>
3009: “I know that I must either find
3010: a way to move beyond the theistic patterns of the past in search for
3011: a new way to speak of and to engage the ultimate reality that I call
3012: God, or be honest about living in a godless world.”  Spong<br>
3013: 	The
3014: question comes up once more: Is one forced into madness or to
3015: relinquish their existence?  Can one confront the Other in an
3016: authentic way that is not motivated by a purely selfish need for
3017: comfort or 'security' within the nightmare of existence?  Can a
3018: language of Nihilism, having the duel roles of navigating the Nothing
3019: of the world while propagating the Transcendental aspect of
3020: meaninglessness, as an authentic way to encounter the Other, be
3021: developed?  Is there any place for philosophical thinking in this
3022: world of contradictions? <br>
3023: <br>
3024: 	“Nevertheless, this
3025: existentially ‘possible’ Being-towards-death remains, from the
3026: existential point of view, a fantastical exaction...Does Dasein ever
3027: factically throw itself into such a Being-towards-death? Does Dasein
3028: demand, even by reason of its own-most Being, an authentic
3029: potentiality-for-Being determined by anticipation?”  Heidegger<br>
3030: <br>
3031: One must first accept that one does not know what one is talking
3032: about. There is not one person who knows what is 'going on here'.
3033: Underhill expresses, fully, the self-criticism that this sort of
