```html
3034: project is bound to: “If the metaphysician be true to his own
3035: postulates, he must acknowledge in the end that we are all forced to
3036: live, to think, and at last to die, in an unknown and unknowable
3037: world: fed arbitrarily and diligently, yet how we know not, by ideas
3038: and suggestions whose truth we cannot test but whose pressure we
3039: cannot resist.” <br>
3040: 	Could we circumnavigate all this and express
3041: the experience of Nihilism in the simplest of terms as: 'all worldly
3042: endeavors are futile'.  It seems that we cannot.  Cliches, as such,
3043: will never do, since, as Heidegger points out, cliches are nothing
3044: but a form of empty 'idle talk' riddled with 'ambiguity' of a passing
3045: off as 'knowing'.  This 'passing over' is simply another distraction.
3046:  There may be a 'message' that is missed when we speak of Nihilism in
3047: such cursory ways.  If we want to develop a language of Nihilism that
3048: is not simply Naturalistic, that is beyond the dead 'solutions' of
3049: the worldly philosophies and religions, and moreover, that is not
3050: simply idle-talk that contributes nothing more than to a premature
3051: suicide, then we must proceed past such banalities.<br>
3052: 	A main
3053: problem with discussing Nihilism is that there are no words that hold
3054: a tight enough grasp on the experience itself as to allow the
3055: experience to become transferable.  St. Therese of Lisieux explains
3056: her experience within a Christian structure: “He allowed my soul to
3057: be overwhelmed with darkness, and the thought of Heaven, which had
3058: consoled me from my earliest childhood, now became a subject of
3059: conflict and torture...I wish I could express what I feel, but it is
3060: beyond me. One must have passed through this dark tunnel to
3061: understand its blackness.”<br>
3062: 	Is one who has experienced
3063: Nihilism, with its essence of 'non-transferability', now left to a
3064: Quietist</div>
3065: <div>
3066: silence?  I believe that an honest answer to this question is: yes. 
3067: Do we stop here?  We cannot, because we are still in the theoretical.
3068:  Most, of the seemingly rare few, who have experienced Nihilism do
3069: not have the 'stamina' to renounce the world in its entirety. 
3070: Experiencing Nothing and renouncing everything are not equivalent (as
3071: we spoke of in the section on Naturalism).  Beyond renunciation, even
3072: the Suicide, who claims to feel a 'brand' of Nihilism, is seemingly
3073: misunderstanding the message.  The Nihilist is one who uses suicide,
3074: rather than commits it; at least for a particular span of time.  <br>
3075: 	Is
3076: there then some type of 'affirmative' substance that is found within
3077: the experience of Nihilism that allows us to move forward in a task
3078: of developing a language that breaks through what could otherwise be
3079: perceived as a simple pointing out, or culmination, of the 'world's
3080: sufferings'?  To quote Cioran once more on the ' affirmative
3081: substance' of Nothing: “The initial revelation of any monastery:
3082: everything is nothing.  Thus begin all mysticisms.  It is less than
3083: one step from nothing to God, for God is the positive expression of
3084: nothingness.”<br>
3085: 	A language of Nihilism reflects the need to
3086: confront the Transcendental yearning as a true confrontation, rather
3087: than something that one tries to replace with this or that thing,
3088: this or that creed, this or that activity, etc.  The need for such a
3089: language in demonstrated in the words of Huxley: “...the ultimate
3090: Reality is not clearly and immediately apprehended, except by those
3091: who have made themselves loving, pure in heart and poor in spirit.
3092: This being so, it is hardly surprising that a theology based upon the
3093: experience of nice, ordinary, unregenerate people should carry so
3094: little conviction.  This kind of empirical theology is on precisely
3095: the same footing as an empirical astronomy, based upon the experience
3096: of naked-eye observers.”<br>
3097: 	A 'new language' of Nihilism is one
3098: that must be worked out in order to provide a more adequate account
3099: of the Transcendence of and within human experience, as revealed
3100: through Nihilism, without specifically holding to either a purely
3101: 'religious' or 'naturalist' worldview.  With great reservation, I
3102: suggest the use of the word Nihiltheism as a word to ground this
3103: 'genre' of human experience.  This is a language that is not strictly
3104: based upon on myth, tradition, nor empirical facts.  This language
3105: must also not ever be taken as 'complete' ('complete' in a similar
3106: way in which 'literal' would be used with regards to interpreting a
3107: mythical language), but rather as a symbolic language which is always
3108: self-critical and therefore always open for re-interpretation.<br>
3109: 	A
3110: language, as any other, may be able to be developed in order to
3111: 'navigate' the 'complexities' of Nihilism in both its forms, as the
3112: Nothing of the world and its Transcendence.  The groundwork for a
3113: language of Nihilism, at least up till the present moment, is found
3114: in its most 'complete' form in Heidegger's comprehensive language
3115: about the human condition as described in Being and Time.  <br>
3116: 	Within
3117: the 'already-made' language of Heidegger we have concepts of anxiety,
3118: moments of vision, everydayness, the call of conscience, the
3119: situation, authenticity, uncanniness, resoluteness, and so on,
3120: already laid out and defined, that capture key insights into the
3121: human condition.  These insights and concepts allow for an
3122: interpretation of the Nihilistic experience on 'non-naturalistic'
3123: terms while still being grounded within the naturalistic aspect of
3124: the human condition.  <br>
3125: 	There is no need to get bogged down with
3126: Heidegger's perspective being essentially 'atheistic' or ' theistic'
3127: in itself, since we are specifically looking to interpret and
3128: discover a language in which a Nihilist may speak about the human
3129: situation as he experiences it.  Heidegger himself attempts to avoid
3130: such presumptuous terms as 'atheistic' or 'theistic' as they are
3131: taken to be ultimately restrictive for a full analysis of the human
3132: situation.  What Heidegger speaks of 'objectively' must still be
3133: experienced subjectively.  Therefore, Heidegger's analysis may only
3134: be seen as an introductory 'map' with regards to speaking about the
3135: Other.  This 'map' need not guarantee a 'direct' path to the Other. 
3136: In other words, assuming there can be such a thing as a 'language of
3137: Nihilism', the possible interpretations may vary immensely.  This
3138: 'Other' is an aspect of the developing language that will have to
3139: rest in obscurity, not only in these introductory words, but even
3140: within a more developed speech-set.  <br>
3141: 	Without the need, yet, to
3142: go into detail concerning all of Heidegger's terms or concepts, a
3143: bare-bones laying out of the basic structure may be put down in order
3144: to help envision the coherency of Heidegger's analysis of human
3145: existence as a possible foundation for developing a language of
3146: Nihilism.  Therefore, a very brief summary of what Heidegger refers
3147: to as 'Being-Towards-Death' with some of its particularities will be
3148: put forth as an example of the foundations of such a language. 
3149: 'Being-Towards-Death', concisely put, consist of anxiety, in a moment
3150: of vision, having a call of conscience that seems to come 'from me
3151: and beyond me' (Buber- “It appears simultaneously as acting on and
3152: as acted upon”), that 'gives something to be understood' in an
3153: 'indefinite', incommunicable way, and which causes the world to 'drop
3154: out' due everything in it sinking into insignificant, yet which
3155: pushes one to strive to stay resolute in this anxiety produced
3156: experience of knowing that death may happen at any moment, which, as
3157: a result, allows one to escape the 'everydayness' of the world in
3158: order to turn inward with attempts to discover one's True-Self.
3159: Without even going into detail, Heidegger's structure is one which
3160: should not be difficult to see how his analysis of the human
3161: condition explicitly lends itself to the building of a Nihilistic
3162: language.  <br>
3163: 	Is Heidegger's, commonly labeled as 'atheistic',
3164: analysis really at all different from what the Trappist monk, Thomas
3165: Merton, has come to accept?  Merton also clearly states the
3166: bankruptcy of a constant outward-seeking, hectic search for 'meaning'
3167: within life.  In comparison to Heidegger, he express the same idea in
3168: this language: “The truly sacred attitude toward life is in no
3169: sense an escape from the sense of nothingness that assails us when we
3170: are left alone with ourselves.  On the contrary, it penetrates into
3171: that darkness and that nothingness, realizing that the mercy of God
3172: has transformed our nothingness into His temple and believing that in
3173: our darkness His light has hidden itself. Hence the sacred attitude
3174: is one which does not recoil from our own inner emptiness, but rather
3175: penetrates it with awe and reverence, and with the awareness of
3176: mystery. This is a most important discovery in, the interior life.”
3177:  Is the use of the word 'God' enough to somehow make any sort of
3178: distinction between Heidegger's 'atheism' (which he explicitly
3179: denies) and Merton's 'theism'?  A Nihilistic language would be one
3180: that attempts to reconcile such superficial disparities. <br>
3181: 	With
3182: that said, Heidegger admits that his full conception of
3183: 'Being-Towards-Death' is one that is 'fantastical'.  That is to say,
3184: if we are to develop past a purely Heideggarian analysis of Being,
3185: how does one, in fact, proceed in a constant participation with an
3186: existence that is drowned in the meaninglessness of the world and
3187: which faces annihilation at any moment?  Is it possible to even
3188: proceed with, or in, such a 'fantastical' structure?  Further, what
3189: is to be meant by a 'True-Self' as interpreted on not 'wholly
3190: naturalistic' terms which avoids, in its analysis of the human
3191: condition, from falling back into the language of myth and worldly
3192: 'religion'?  On the other hand, is there any kind of 'positive'
3193: substance that stems from the Nihilistic experience which is not to
3194: be interpreted as wholly biological, naturalistic, and worldly, as
3195: many interpreters of Heidegger have done so far with his language? 
3196: Heidegger himself dismisses both 'biological' explanations and,
3197: seemingly traditional conceptions of, 'God' as too preemptive as they
3198: are hindered by assumptions that do not necessarily leave open the
3199: mystery of the 'Why?'. <br>
3200: <br>
3201: 	"The onotological analysis
3202: of conscience on which we are thus embarking, is prior to any
3203: description and classification of Experiences of conscience, and
3204: likewise lies outsuide any biological 'explination' of this
3205: phenomenon.  But it is also no less distant from a theological
3206: exigesis of conscience or any employment of this phenomenon for
3207: proofs of God or  for establishing an 'immediate' conscience of God."
3208:  (pg. 313)<br>
3209: <br>
3210: 	('The Call' as both from oneself and beyond
3211: oneself)  "If the interpretation continues in this direction,
3212: one supplies a possesor for the power thus posited, or one takes the
3213: power itself as a person who makes himself known- namely God.  On the
3214: other hand one may try to reject this explination in which the caller
3215: is taken as an alien manifestation of such a power, and to explain
3216: away the conscience 'biologically' at the same time.  Both these
3217: sexplinations pass over the phenomenal findings too hastly."
3218: (pg. 320)<br>
3219: <br>
3220: 	This new language must not be seen as
3221: 'rescuing' one from the Nothingness, rather it is a language that
3222: attempts to bring one 'face to face' with the nightmare of existence.
3223:  The terror of existence is one that opens up the 'positive' content.
3224:  The unending questioning of 'why?' opens up something that is not
3225: wholly natural, yet must still be experienced and discussed within
3226: the world.  Even the mystical experience, while a blow to Naturalism,
3227: does not defeat Naturalism.  One of the few accurate statements that
3228: Sartre put forth when describing his particular brand of 
3229: 'existentialism' was that whether God exists or not, it's all still
3230: the same, it makes no difference (regardless</div>
3231: <div> if
3232: he was using this statement as a 'defense' of his atheistic
3233: existentialism).  The ultimately empty structure of Naturalism must
3234: be experienced as a ladder into Transcendence, not as an obstacle to
3235: be overcome.  One must not play with the toys of the world that are
3236: crushed into dust with every movement. To keep grabbing at new,
3237: vanishing toys is but a distraction of unthinking robots that is not
3238: grounded in Nihilism.<br>
3239: 	When one holds to any form of optimism as
3240: they speak of the Other, one knows that this person has not been
3241: grasped by the Nothing of the world.  While we are all damned to some
3242: particular idolatry, optimism is one of its lowest forms.  The
3243: fanaticism of thought that brews in the fear of Nihilism is only a
3244: turning away from Nihilism, an illusion of comfort; there is no
3245: embracing, there is no participation in the Nothingness.  The worldly
3246: religions are fundamentally fanatical in nature due to their turning
3247: away of Nihilism.  Spong, in his desire to 'revive' the dying
3248: language of Christianity, says: “We fear that if theism is
3249: dismissed, only a bottomless pit remains.” and “Many forms of
3250: religion are little more than cultural manifestations of the fear of
3251: nothingness.  That is why people become hysterical when theism is
3252: challenged.”  <br>
3253: 	 One last mention on the falsity of 'optimism'
3254: is deserving, if only because it is the overwhelming strain of
3255: thought within the current American culture; this 'optimism' shows
3256: itself in forms of 'prosperity churches' all the way to some type of
3257: foundationless 'humanism'.  Quoting St. Seraphim Rose once more will
3258: thus prove to be deserving: “And indeed the Christian is, in a
3259: certain sense--in an ultimate sense--a "Nihilist"; for to
3260: him, in the end, the world is nothing, and God is all. This is, of
3261: course, the precise opposite of the Nihilism we have examined here,
3262: where God is nothing and the world is all; that is a Nihilism that
3263: proceeds from the Abyss, and the Christian's is a "Nihilism"
3264: that proceeds from abundance. The true Nihilist places his faith in
3265: things that pass away and end in nothing; all "optimism" on
3266: this foundation is clearly futile.”<br>
3267: 	A relevant example of
3268: this sort of 'unthinking', or lack of search for Transcendence, can
3269: be shown within the mindset of a majority of practicing scientists of
3270: today.  Science, assuming a 'realist' perspective, as describing
3271: 'reality' with physical laws, allows one to predict and 'know' what
3272: is or will happen within the world.  However, to stop at this formal
3273: description of reality is unphilosophical.  The Philosopher wants to
3274: ask why the descriptions of science are the way they are.  It is
3275: 'understood' that 'particles' and 'molecules' bump into one another
3276: in a particular pattern which produce the physical objects that we
3277: see around us and even ourselves.  To comprehend a description of
3278: things is not to comprehend why, seemingly mindless particles and
3279: molecules are acting in the ways in which they do.  Why are there
3280: 'things', why are these 'things' following a particular rational
3281: structure, and why is a there a 'building' process found within these
3282: 'things'?  These philosophical questions are shunned by a majority of
3283: mainstream scientific 'intellectuals'.  This sort of 'unthinking' is
3284: what has produced the 'optimistic' scientism of the current times. 
3285: <br>
3286: 	There is a similar criticism of 'unthinking' to be made
3287: against traditional 'religious' interpretations of the human
3288: condition.  Many religious interpretations of the human experience
3289: are replete with theological assumptions that are not necessarily
3290: grounded in human experience; these take the forms of rituals,
3291: prayers, moral declarations, metaphysical claims, and so on. 
3292: However, a language that prepares 'answers' or 'rational defenses'
3293: rather than opening up the mystery of the ground of all things must
3294: be labeled as a lower form of idolatry.  These assumptions, as with
3295: many religious languages, are the reason why Heidegger proclaimed
3296: that a 'Christian philosophy' was the equivalent of a 'round-square'.
3297:  Heidegger's thought is that the religious languages that have so far
3298: been developed are restrictive of the human experience of the Other. 
3299: In other words, religions with their theologies contain too many
3300: assumptions and 'answers' that shun the mystery of existence. 
3301: Philosophy, on the other hand, the relentless 'Why?', is what is
3302: needed in order to open up the mystery of Being, instead of closing
3303: it off and pushing it into a closet.  One cannot turn the Other into
3304: the worldly, whether that attempt is through reason or myth.  These
3305: are simple, lower forms of idolatry. <br>
3306: <br>
3307: <br>
3308: “In
3309: particular, that in the face of which one has anxiety is not
3310: encountered as something definite with which one can concern oneself;
3311: the threatening does not come from what is ready-to-hand or
3312: present-at-hand, but rather from the fact that neither of these
3313: ‘says’ anything any longer. Environmental entities no longer have
3314: any involvement. The world in which I exist has sunk into
3315: insignificance; and the world which is thus disclosed is one in which
3316: entities can be freed only in the character of having no involvement.
3317: Anxiety is anxious in the face of the “nothing” of the world; but
3318: this does not mean that in anxiety we experience something like the
3319: absence of what is present-at-hand within-the-world. The
3320: present-at-hand must be encountered in just such a way that it does
3321: not have any involvement whatsoever, but can show itself in an empty
3322: mercilessness.”  68b<br>
3323: <br>
3324: “Anxiety discloses the
3325: insignificance of the world; and this insignificance reveals the
3326: nullity of that with which one can concern oneself—or, in other
3327: words, the impossibility of projecting oneself upon a
3328: potentiality-for-Being which belongs to existence and which is
3329: founded primarily upon one’s objects of concern.”<br>
3330: <br>
3331: “That
3332: in the face of which one is anxious is completely indefinite. Not
3333: only does this indefiniteness leave factically undecided which entity
3334: within-the-world is threatening us, but it also tells us that
3335: entities within-the-world are not ‘relevant’ at all.”<br>
3336: <br>
3337: “The
3338: utter insignificance which makes itself known in the 'nothing and
3339: nowhere', does not signify that the world is absent, but tells us
3340: that entities within-the-world are of so little importance in
3341: themselves that on the basis of this insignificance of what is
3342: within-the-world, the world in its worldhood is all that still
3343: obtrudes itself.”<br>
3344: <br>
3345: “The question is there in a spell of
3346: boredom, when we are equally distant from despair and joy, but when
3347: the stubborn ordinariness of beings lays open a wasteland in which it
3348: makes no difference to us whether beings are or are not—and then,
3349: in a distinctive form, the question resonates once again: Why are
3350: there beings at all instead of nothing?”<br>
3351: <br>
3352: “In dread,
3353: being as a whole becomes untenable.” <br>
3354: <br>
3355: “That existence
3356: is pervaded by nihilating behavior attests to the permanent and
3357: indeed obscured manifestness of no-thing that dread originally
3358: discloses. But this means original dread is suppressed for the most
3359: part in existence. Dread is there. It's only napping. Its breath
3360: permanently trembles in existence, only slightly in the apprehensive,
3361: and inaudibly in the "Uh húh!" and "Húh uh!" of
3362: those who are busy; best of all in the reserved, surest of all at the
3363: heart of existence that is daring. But this happens only in those for
3364: whom it expends itself in order to preserve the ultimate greatness of
3365: existence.”<br>
3366: <br>
3367: “The beholdenness of existence to no-thing
3368: on the basis of hidden dread is the surmounting of being as a whole,
3369: transcendence.”<br>
3370: <br>
3371: “As surely as we never get a sure
3372: grasp of the generality of be-ing in itself, just assurely do we all
3373: the same find ourselves somehow placed in the midst of the generality
3374: of bare be-ing. In the end, there continues to be [besteht]an
3375: essential difference between getting a grasp of the whole of be-ing
3376: in itself and finding oneself in the midst of be-ing as a whole.  The
3377: former is impossible in principle. The latter happens all the time in
3378: our existence. Of course, it looks just as though in our everyday
3379: comings and goings we were holding fast to only just this or that
3380: [kind of] be-ing, as though we were lost in this or that realm of
3381: be-ing. But no matter how fragmented the daily round may seem, it
3382: always maintains be-ing in the unity of a "whole," although
3383: only in the shadows.  Even then and precisely just then, when we are
3384: not especially busy with things, this "as a whole"
3385: overcomes us; for example, in genuine boredom.  This is a long way
3386: off far off when this or that book or play, job or leisure activity,
3387: is boring. It breaks out when "it's boring." Profound
3388: boredom, like a silent fog insinuating itself in the depths of
3389: existence, pulls things, others and oneself into it altogether with
3390: remarkable indifference. Such boredom reveals be-ing as a whole.”<hr><h1><a href="upnote://x-callback-url/tag/view?tag=Nihiltheism" data-non-editable="true" spellcheck="false" data-upnote-tag="#Nihiltheism" title="#Nihiltheism">#Nihiltheism</a><a href="upnote://x-callback-url/tag/view?tag=direxperience" data-non-editable="true" spellcheck="false" data-upnote-tag="#direxperience" title="#direxperience">#direxperience</a><a href="upnote://x-callback-url/tag/view?tag=naturalism" data-non-editable="true" spellcheck="false" data-upnote-tag="#naturalism" title="#mystical" data-upnote-tag="#mystical" spellcheck="false" data-non-editable="true" href="upnote://x-callback-url/tag/view?tag=mystical">#mystical</a>&nbsp;<a title="#langauge" data-upnote-tag="#langauge" spellcheck="false" data-non-editable="true" href="upnote://x-callback-url/tag/view?tag=langauge">#langauge</a></h1><div><a data-non-editable="true" Nihilism</a></div><div><a data-non-editable="true" data-note-id="4424cfcd-68fd-40a4-a127-13835f2d6384" href="upnote://x-callback-url/openNote?noteId=4424cfcd-68fd-40a4-a127-13835f2d6384">The Uncanny Illusion of Naturalism</a></div><div><a data-non-editable="true" data-note-id="397b60f2-0c4b-4825-bdb2-ca81b3eff7e9" data-note-id="fc9e2d1d-fdcb-4bd5-ba73-4286c1716bb3" href="upnote://x-callback-url/openNote?noteId=fc9e2d1d-fdcb-4bd5-ba73-4286c1716bb3">The Startling Encounter with Infinite Nothingness</a></div><div><a data-non-editable="true" data-note-id="aaae8c71-8355-4c08-ac6b-104854227cb0" href="upnote://x-callback-url/openNote?noteId=aaae8c71-8355-4c08-ac6b-104854227cb0">The Symbolic Resonance of Nothing</a></div><div><br></div><div><a data-non-editable="true" data-note-id="e522d18f-dd1c-47ca-ad66-78f9ba9a4b43" 3391: </html>
```

1-1.181.001V2.954h-.887v5.613a1.478 1.478 0 0 0 2.954 0V2.364a2.363 2.363 0 1 0-4.727 0V9.75C0 11.546 1.454 13 3.25 13S6.5 11.546 6.5 9.75V2.955z'/%3E%3C/svg%3E");padding:0 5px}.shine-editor 7: </head>
8: <body class="light-theme"><div id="app"><div id="editor"><div id="shine-editor" class="shine-editor"><div><h2>You’ve Ruined My Life, Professor Craig</h2><div
Dear Professor Craig
</div><div>
My name is Adam. I am an atheist, and have been one ever since I can remember. I was brought up in the Roman Catholic church, but could never really say that I held any theistic beliefs with any sincerity. For instance, one time, when I was around eight or nine years old, I asked a nun at my church where I "was" before I was born. She responded: "You were with God." I was still curious, so I asked her how long I was with God, before my being born, and she proclaimed: "For an eternity!" I then asked her why I could not remember "existing with God” for an eternity of time (is an eternity of time even a coherent concept?). She had enough with that question and proceeded to shew me away to play with the other children. Looking back, I am proud of my skeptical disposition.</div><div><br></div><div>Let's skip ahead a little bit. I found philosophy and I fell in love with it. I transferred schools in order to obtain my BA. Almost all of the papers I wrote as an undergrad were about atheism or God. I was on a mission to be as rational as I could with regards to my atheistic beliefs. Moreover, I was practically an "evangelical" atheist, proclaiming the good word of rationality! My beliefs were strident at best, and intolerant at worst. I thought I had the "God question" all figured out. It was a settled issue for me: God did not exist. The philosophy of religion was my initial and main draw to philosophy, but I soon found myself wanting to explore philosophy in all its glory. Philosophy, as a whole, was too interesting to just "stop", then move on to some "real job". I decided to apply to an MA program in philosophy at CSULA, and got accepted. Philosophy was something that I took very seriously. So much so, that I drove from NY to CA with no job and no place to live in order to continue my studies. I actually wrote a response to your paper&nbsp;<em>The Absurdity of Life Without God</em>&nbsp;and used it as my writing sample in order to get in to CSULA. I stayed up for months writing and polishing my responses to your claims of the inconsistency of atheism in its response to meaning, value, and purpose in life. I had too. You were telling me my life, as a&nbsp;<em>direct</em>&nbsp;result of my worldview, was worthless in every possible way. Well, as an ambitious philosophy student, I could not simply let you get away this. Your objections to atheism needed answers. And after wrestling with your paper for some time, I actually felt pretty good about the end product and presumed to have "answered" your objections to atheism in a satisfactory way. I could now move on, live my life with the excitement, consistency, and appreciation that I had before reading your essay.</div><div><br></div><div>I was wrong.</div><div><br></div><div>I should have known better too, since the first time I read that paper of yours, I couldn't sleep for two days. It completely shattered my worldview. Let me mention here that I was a huge fan of the New Atheists, but I always sensed something was askew with them. Something seemed off about them because whenever they were talking about meaning, value, or purpose, they answered in such ways that only a person ignorant of the objections in your paper could respond. In short (too late), your paper never left my mind, even years after I wrote a "response" to it. I knew, deep down, that not only did I not, but could not answer your objections to atheism. What you say the atheistic worldview entails is true. There is no escaping the nihilism as an atheist.</div><div><br></div><div>Everything has died for me.</div><div><br></div><div>You have ruined my life.</div><div><br></div><div>Before I go any further, let me say that you are and always have been my favorite living philosopher. I have seen every debate you have ever recorded and put up on the internet. I watch all your lectures and talks (Closer to Truth, youtube, etc.) I think you are the epitome of what a philosopher should be. You're uber logical, fantastically clear, and "computeresk" with the speed and precision of your responses to objections against your position, particularly the criticisms you respond to in your debates. For a long time now, I have wanted to be a philosopher as you are a philosopher. I want to have an argument posed against my position, and be able to dissect it in the same manner as you do. I can honestly say that I have learned more from reading what you have wrote and watching you on the internet than maybe all of my years in school, formally studying philosophy. I seem to owe you a lot, with regards to my philosophical development, at least.</div><div><br></div><div>Now, let's get down to business and why exactly it is that you have ruined my life. After reading your paper on the absurdity of life without God, I soon realized that I&nbsp;<em>had</em>&nbsp;to become a nihilist. To act otherwise would inevitably reduce into an inconsistency. Nihilism is the logical conclusion of an atheistic worldview. Yet, nihilism is unlivable. Christopher Hitchens used to say that you cannot derive any knowledge of what an atheist believes from the fact that she is an atheist. If someone claims to be an atheist, according to Hitchens, you can only conclude that she believes that "God does not exist" or that she "lacks a belief in God" (don't get me started with that distinction!!!) You cannot “go any further” and know if she is a Marxist or a Capitalist, etc. But your paper shows that Hitchens is patently false about this. Atheism necessarily entails nihilistic conclusions about certain questions, particularly those you bring up in your paper about meaning, value, and purpose.</div><div><br></div><div>There is a similar misunderstanding that shows how atheists fail to fully comprehend the severity of their own worldview. I feel like I need to say how disappointed I am in the New Atheists, and moreover, the professional philosophers, who do not understand the "moral argument" for God's existence. Why can't they comprehend the ontology of values? Why is this so difficult? It is all too obvious that you are not talking about whether people can act, or know of the, "good" on atheism, but rather that there is no foundation for morality outside of God. Sorry, I just had to rant a little, because it bothers me when philosophers, who should know better, don't comprehend the moral argument. I can only imagine how frustrated you must feel. Further, I hate all the nasty comments you get on YouTube. People don't even understand how well thought out your views are. You have the most coherent worldview I have ever heard anyone describe. Sorry, just wanted to say that you have at least one atheist on your side, sir.</div><div>So, this brings me to the problem (finally, sorry)-</div><div><br></div><div>Philosophically, I agree with almost everything you say. Not in a “follower” sense, but in that I find what you say either convincingly true or I find I come to the same conclusions that you do with regards to particular ideas that I have reasoned through on my own. With that said, I am still an atheist. How is this possible? How could my favorite philosopher be a Christian, I agree with almost everything he says, yet I am an atheist? Well, it seems that you make an extremely strong case for the rationality of an “abstract” notion of God, but I cannot get myself to go the extra step further and believe any of the world religions (not that I believe this abstract God actually exists either, it just seems to be becoming more and more plausible to me). I definitely cannot get myself to be a Christian. Christianity just does not seem true to me. However, the deeper I dive into philosophy, the more the theistic worldview seems more plausible. The concepts or “language” of mathematics seems to “cry out” as you put it for an explanation, objective moral values seem to be real (but they can't be “real”, if atheism is true), the idea of "existence" nauseates me to no end (just the thought of anything, at all, existing, and especially existing without any reason, frightens me,), and I could go on and on. You know, all the things you speak about in your YouTube videos.</div><div><br></div><div>However, even when I don't think about the arguments, and I think about what you and others have said about the “Holy Spirit”, I cannot get myself to believe that this “Holy Spirit” exists and can authenticate my belief in God. As you know, Martin Luther thought the Holy Spirit would guide people in reading the Bible properly, when the Reformation was underway, since there was the concern that without the guidance of the church, people may interpret the bible incorrectly. The Holy Spirit's guiding capabilities seem to be have been proven empirically false though, due to such a wide variety of conflicting beliefs all being derived from the Bible. Now I know that the diversity of beliefs doesn't necessarily allow for the conclusion that all of the religious beliefs or experiences are false, that there aren't at least some beliefs or experiences that are true, and therefore the Holy Spirit does not exist. However, this disagreement does cause pause in me, and it makes the situation suspect enough to where I cannot seemingly distinguish between an authentic experience with the Holy Spirit or my being deluded. There does not seem to be anything here to help me out of the nihilistic rut. Lately though, I have been questioning why I even value “rationality”, or what it even means to “value” rationality on an atheistic worldview. That's another topic though, sorry.</div><div><br></div><div>Let me wrap this ramble up. I am now stuck in a nihilistic-atheistic world that I hate. Agnosticism is not even a coherent position to me, with regards to a Perfect Being, since I believe that the greatest conceivable being could give me knowledge of its existence, if it wanted to. Theism is a dream come true. The world would make sense, the existential mysteries that haunt me would be solved, life would be livable. It is atheism, however, which seems to be true, yet I do not want to live like this. I have become depressed to no end. I have been in a nihilistic rut for years now. I have become utterly recluse. Yet, even with all this, I cannot come to believe in God. What would YOU suggest I do? This letter is as sincere as it gets. You may be my last hope. Since I agree with you on so much, I was hoping you would have the answer to this. I know the “answer”&nbsp;<em>is</em>&nbsp;Christianity, but as I said, I cannot get myself to believe its truth.&nbsp;</div><div><br></div><div>I am an atheist who hates atheism. I want there to be a God more than anything, yet I cannot get myself to believe in one. I cannot seem to give an adequate answer to Camus's question: “Is life worth living?”</div><div>A feel like a philosopher of your caliber is the only person I have left to turn to. A psychologist wouldn't get my concerns, at least I don't think think they would. I need the clarity and reasonableness of a philosopher. Please, help.</div><div>Your biggest atheist fan,</div><div>Adam</div><div><br></div><div>PS- Do me one favor? Even if you never have time to respond. Please, do not debate Lawrence Krauss anymore. As with most people not trained in philosophy, it is not even an argument in any sort of philosophical sense. He is a shouting bag of hot air with irrelevant criticisms and gross misunderstandings of the arguments. He does not deserve to be the face for atheism, we both know this. But maybe this is your plan.&nbsp;
<img width="19" src="http://www.reasonablefaith.org/images/smileys/smile.gif" alt="smile"></div><div><img alt="Flag of United States." src="https://www.reasonablefaith.org/images/site/flags/united_states.png">&nbsp;United States</div></div><div><img alt="Photo of Dr. Craig." src="https://www.reasonablefaith.org/images/site/a2drcraig-img-1.jpg"><h2>DR. CRAIG’S RESPONSE</h2><hr>Adam,&nbsp;</div><div>it is so encouraging to get a letter like yours! You have seen through the fog of often hateful rhetoric to discern the real issues. Your story reminds me so much of C. S. Lewis, who, as a scientific naturalist, found everything that he thought was real to be meaningless and unfulfilling, and everything he loved, such as myth, legend, and fantasy, to be imaginary and unreal. Like you, Lewis approached Christ gradually, shedding first his naturalism and then later his scepticism about Jesus of Nazareth. Lewis finally broke the bonds of the naturalism that had bound him, discovering in Christ the “true myth,” the fusion of rationality and imagination. Once you free yourself of your atheism, you’ll find the next step to Christian theism a relatively short one.<div><br></div><div>You mention your scepticism about Luther’s belief that the Holy Spirit “<em>would guide people in reading the Bible properly</em>,” for “<em>The Holy Spirit's guiding capabilities seem to be have been proven empirically false. . . , due to such a wide variety of conflicting beliefs all being derived from the Bible.</em>” Now I don’t subscribe to the belief you attribute to Luther; nevertheless, the argument you offer against it does not strike me as a good one. In order to prevent a plethora of divergent interpretations, what would be required is, not just that Holy Spirit would guide people into the truth, but that Holy Spirit would intervene to prevent aberrant interpretations of the Bible as well, and Luther never claimed that He does that. If Luther is right, one would expect to find people adhering to a correct interpretation and others departing from the true way and propounding deviant interpretations, which is exactly what we do find. Luther’s view would be falsified only if everyone departed from the truth.</div><div><br></div><div>I think that what the Holy Spirit does is to furnish believers with a fundamental assurance of their being properly related to God and unbelievers a conviction of their not being properly related to God but of being in need of His forgiveness and moral cleansing. While this entails the truth of certain core Christian claims, it does not guarantee proper interpretation of biblical passages. That is instead achieved through the use of proper hermeneutical techniques of literary interpretation.</div><div><br></div><div>I think you already discern the weakness of your objection, for you say, “<em>I know that the diversity of beliefs doesn't necessarily allow for the conclusion that all of the religious beliefs or experiences are false, that there aren't at least some beliefs or experiences that are true, and therefore the Holy Spirit does not exist.</em>” Right; you need to be open to the convicting witness of the Spirit in your life that you need God’s forgiveness and cleansing for your moral failures. Anyone who thinks hard about the moral argument should realize that if objective moral values and duties do exist, he falls desperately short of the moral good and so fails to discharge his moral duties and is thus in need of forgiveness and redemption. Indeed, I think probably no one comes to faith in Christ apart from a conviction of his guilt and need of moral renovation.</div><div><br></div><div>You have good things to say about the theistic arguments I’ve defended; but you do not mention my equally rigorous work, flowing out of my doctoral studies under Wolfhart Pannenberg at the University of Munich, on the historicity of Jesus’ resurrection. I was astonished to discover as a result of my study that the main facts undergirding the historicity of Jesus’ resurrection are actually agreed upon by the majority of historical Jesus scholars today, not just conservative scholars but the broad mainstream of New Testament scholars, including a good number of Jewish scholars, who teach at secular universities and non-evangelical divinity schools. So I think faith in Jesus is historically quite well-founded.</div><div><br></div><div>So now we come to the million dollar question: “<em>What would YOU suggest I do?</em>” I take this question very seriously. So here’s what I suggest you do:&nbsp;<br><br></div><div><ul><li> Read C. S. Lewis’ book&nbsp;<em>Surprised by Joy</em>. I think you will resonate with Lewis’ struggle, both to free himself of atheism and then of his scepticism about Christian faith.</li><li>&nbsp;Seek experiences that put you in touch with the transcendent. You need to escape the cloying bonds of naturalism by catching glimpses of a transcendent reality beyond the material world. This will help to prepare your heart for belief in God. So open yourself to experiences of sublime beauty. Listen to&nbsp;<a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=fHlfNYY1YIY">Schumann’s&nbsp;<em>Träumerei</em></a>, to&nbsp;<a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=ETNoPqYAIPI">Dvorak’s&nbsp;<em>New World Symphony</em></a>, to&nbsp;<a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=s_pkRH2DZuw">Rimsky-Korsakov’s&nbsp;<em>Scheherazade</em></a>, and so on. And when I say “listen,” I don’t mean to have it playing in the background while you go about your tasks. I mean to set all else aside for an allotted time, close your eyes, and just focus on listening to the music. Watch a video of ballroom champions Jonathan Crossley and Lyn Marriner performing a&nbsp;<a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=pbLHVJpCaUo">waltz</a>&nbsp;or&nbsp;<a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=wnOHtMJSBjQ">slow foxtrot</a>. Drink in the stunning beauty of their performance. Watch a sunrise or sunset over a beautiful landscape or take in the beauty of pristine nature. Such beauty can sometimes produce an almost painful ache in us because of our inability to take it all in.<br></li></ul><ul><li>Read the Gospels about the life of Jesus. If you haven’t already done so, you’ll find the story of his life gripping. Jesus is a tremendously attractive person in the wisdom of his teaching, in his character, and in the authenticity of his life.<br></li></ul><ul><li>&nbsp;Look into the historical credibility of the personal claims and resurrection of Jesus. Read,&nbsp;<em>e.g</em>., the relevant chapters in&nbsp;<a href="https://apps.biola.edu/apologetics-store/products/books/item/reasonable-faith-christian-truth-and-apologetics-3rd-edition_Book"><em>Reasonable Faith</em></a>&nbsp;(Crossway, 2008). Read my debates with sceptical New Testament critics like John Dominic Crossan (<a href="https://apps.biola.edu/apologetics-store/products/books/item/will-the-real-jesus-please-stand-up_Book"><em>Will the Real Jesus Please Stand Up?,</em></a>&nbsp;ed. Paul Copan [Baker, 1998] or Gerd Lüdemann (<a href="https://apps.biola.edu/apologetics-store/products/books/item/jesus-resurrection-fact-or-figment_Book"><em>The Resurrection: Fact or Figment?</em></a>&nbsp;ed. Paul Copan [Inter-Varsity, 2000] and ask which way the evidence points.<br></li></ul><ul><li>Embark on a spiritual experiment. Begin to pray daily. Attend a church where the Gospel is faithfully preached and you can be with Christians to get to know them. You’ll find these people to be unlike ordinary people you meet, more reflective, more compassionate, more focused on spiritual things.<br></li></ul><ul><li>&nbsp;Finally, get a copy of Francis Thompson’s poem&nbsp;<a href="http://www.ewtn.com/library/HUMANITY/HNDHVN.HTM">“The Hound of Heaven.”</a>&nbsp;The person it describes is you, Adam! “With unhurrying chase, and unperturbèd pace, deliberate speed, majestic instancy,” He is after you and will continue His pursuit until you recognize in Him all that you are longing for.<br></li></ul></div><div><strong><br></strong></div><div><strong>- William Lane Craig</strong></div></div><br><div><a title="#Nihiltheism" data-upnote-tag="#Nihiltheism" spellcheck="false" data-non-editable="true" href="upnote://x-callback-url/tag/view?tag=Nihiltheism">#Nihiltheism</a></div></div></div></div></body>
9:
